 ELEONORAssTHE PURLOINED LETTERssNil sapientiæ odiosius acumine nimio.—_Seneca_.

At Paris, just after dark one gusty evening in the autumn of 18-,ssI was enjoying the twofold luxury of meditation and a meerschaum,sin company with my friend C. Auguste Dupin, in his little backsslibrary, or book-closet, _au troisième_, No. 33, _Rue Dunôt,ssFaubourg St. Germain_. For one hour at least we had maintained assprofound silence; while each, to any casual observer, might havessseemed intently and exclusively occupied with the curling eddiesssof smoke that oppressed the atmosphere of the chamber. Forssmyself, however, I was mentally discussing certain topics whichsshad formed matter for conversation between us at an earlierssperiod of the evening; I mean the affair of the Rue Morgue, andssthe mystery attending the murder of Marie Rogêt. I looked uponssit, therefore, as something of a coincidence, when the door ofssour apartment was thrown open and admitted our old acquaintance,ssMonsieur G——, the Prefect of the Parisian police.ssWe gave him a hearty welcome; for there was nearly half as muchssof the entertaining as of the contemptible about the man, and wesshad not seen him for several years. We had been sitting in thessdark, and Dupin now arose for the purpose of lighting a lamp, butsssat down again, without doing so, upon G.‘s saying that he hadsscalled to consult us, or rather to ask the opinion of my friend,ssabout some official business which had occasioned a great deal ofsstrouble.ss“If it is any point requiring reflection,” observed Dupin, as hessforebore to enkindle the wick, “we shall examine it to bettersspurpose in the dark.”ss“That is another of your odd notions,” said the Prefect, who hadssa fashion of calling every thing “odd” that was beyond hissscomprehension, and thus lived amid an absolute legion ofss“oddities.”ss“Very true,” said Dupin, as he supplied his visitor with a pipe,ssand rolled towards him a comfortable chair.ss“And what is the difficulty now?” I asked. “Nothing more in thessassassination way, I hope?”ss“Oh no; nothing of that nature. The fact is, the business is verysssimple indeed, and I make no doubt that we can manage itsssufficiently well ourselves; but then I thought Dupin would likessto hear the details of it, because it is so excessively odd.”ss“Simple and odd,” said Dupin.ss“Why, yes; and not exactly that, either. The fact is, we have allssbeen a good deal puzzled because the affair is so simple, and yetssbaffles us altogether.”ss“Perhaps it is the very simplicity of the thing which puts you atssfault,” said my friend.ss“What nonsense you do talk!” replied the Prefect, laughingssheartily.ss“Perhaps the mystery is a little too plain,” said Dupin.ss“Oh, good heavens! who ever heard of such an idea?”ss“A little too self-evident.”ss“Ha! ha! ha—ha! ha! ha!—ho! ho! ho!” roared our visitor,ssprofoundly amused, “oh, Dupin, you will be the death of me yet!”ss“And what, after all, is the matter on hand?” I asked.ss“Why, I will tell you,” replied the Prefect, as he gave a long,sssteady and contemplative puff, and settled himself in his chair.ss“I will tell you in a few words; but, before I begin, let messcaution you that this is an affair demanding the greatestsssecrecy, and that I should most probably lose the position I nowsshold, were it known that I confided it to any one.”ss“Proceed,” said I.ss“Or not,” said Dupin.ss“Well, then; I have received personal information, from a verysshigh quarter, that a certain document of the last importance hasssbeen purloined from the royal apartments. The individual whosspurloined it is known; this beyond a doubt; he was seen to takessit. It is known, also, that it still remains in his possession.”ss“How is this known?” asked Dupin.ss“It is clearly inferred,” replied the Prefect, “from the naturessof the document, and from the non-appearance of certain resultssswhich would at once arise from its passing out of the robber’ssspossession; that is to say, from his employing it as he mustssdesign in the end to employ it.”ss“Be a little more explicit,” I said.ss“Well, I may venture so far as to say that the paper gives itsssholder a certain power in a certain quarter where such power isssimmensely valuable.” The Prefect was fond of the cant ofssdiplomacy.ss“Still I do not quite understand,” said Dupin.ss“No? Well; the disclosure of the document to a third person, whossshall be nameless, would bring in question the honor of asspersonage of most exalted station; and this fact gives the holderssof the document an ascendancy over the illustrious personagesswhose honor and peace are so jeopardized.”ss“But this ascendancy,” I interposed, “would depend upon thessrobber’s knowledge of the loser’s knowledge of the robber. Whosswould dare—”ss“The thief,” said G., “is the Minister D——, who dares all things,ssthose unbecoming as well as those becoming a man. The method ofssthe theft was not less ingenious than bold. The document inssquestion—a letter, to be frank—had been received by the personagessrobbed while alone in the royal boudoir. During its perusal shesswas suddenly interrupted by the entrance of the other exaltedsspersonage from whom especially it was her wish to conceal it.ssAfter a hurried and vain endeavor to thrust it in a drawer, shesswas forced to place it, open as it was, upon a table. Thessaddress, however, was uppermost, and, the contents thusssunexposed, the letter escaped notice. At this juncture enters thessMinister D——. His lynx eye immediately perceives the paper,ssrecognises the handwriting of the address, observes the confusionssof the personage addressed, and fathoms her secret. After somessbusiness transactions, hurried through in his ordinary manner, hessproduces a letter somewhat similar to the one in question, opensssit, pretends to read it, and then places it in closessjuxtaposition to the other. Again he converses, for some fifteenssminutes, upon the public affairs. At length, in taking leave, hesstakes also from the table the letter to which he had no claim.ssIts rightful owner saw, but, of course, dared not call attentionssto the act, in the presence of the third personage who stood atssher elbow. The minister decamped; leaving his own letter—one ofssno importance—upon the table.”ss“Here, then,” said Dupin to me, “you have precisely what youssdemand to make the ascendancy complete—the robber’s knowledge ofssthe loser’s knowledge of the robber.”ss“Yes,” replied the Prefect; “and the power thus attained has, forsssome months past, been wielded, for political purposes, to a veryssdangerous extent. The personage robbed is more thoroughlyssconvinced, every day, of the necessity of reclaiming her letter.ssBut this, of course, cannot be done openly. In fine, driven tossdespair, she has committed the matter to me.”ss“Than whom,” said Dupin, amid a perfect whirlwind of smoke, “nossmore sagacious agent could, I suppose, be desired, or evenssimagined.”ss“You flatter me,” replied the Prefect; “but it is possible thatsssome such opinion may have been entertained.”ss“It is clear,” said I, “as you observe, that the letter is stillssin possession of the minister; since it is this possession, andssnot any employment of the letter, which bestows the power. Withssthe employment the power departs.”ss“True,” said G.; “and upon this conviction I proceeded. My firstsscare was to make thorough search of the minister’s hotel; andsshere my chief embarrassment lay in the necessity of searchingsswithout his knowledge. Beyond all things, I have been warned ofssthe danger which would result from giving him reason to suspectssour design.”ss“But,” said I, “you are quite au fait in these investigations.ssThe Parisian police have done this thing often before.”ss“Oh, yes; and for this reason I did not despair. The habits ofssthe minister gave me, too, a great advantage. He is frequentlyssabsent from home all night. His servants are by no meansssnumerous. They sleep at a distance from their master’s apartment,ssand, being chiefly Neapolitans, are readily made drunk. I havesskeys, as you know, with which I can open any chamber or cabinetssin Paris. For three months a night has not passed, during thessgreater part of which I have not been engaged, personally, inssransacking the D—— Hotel. My honor is interested, and, to mentionssa great secret, the reward is enormous. So I did not abandon thesssearch until I had become fully satisfied that the thief is assmore astute man than myself. I fancy that I have investigatedssevery nook and corner of the premises in which it is possiblessthat the paper can be concealed.”ss“But is it not possible,” I suggested, “that although the letterssmay be in possession of the minister, as it unquestionably is, hessmay have concealed it elsewhere than upon his own premises?”ss“This is barely possible,” said Dupin. “The present peculiarsscondition of affairs at court, and especially of those intriguesssin which D—— is known to be involved, would render the instantssavailability of the document—its susceptibility of being producedssat a moment’s notice—a point of nearly equal importance with itssspossession.”ss“Its susceptibility of being produced?” said I.ss“That is to say, of being destroyed,” said Dupin.ss“True,” I observed; “the paper is clearly then upon the premises.ssAs for its being upon the person of the minister, we may considerssthat as out of the question.”ss“Entirely,” said the Prefect. “He has been twice waylaid, as ifssby footpads, and his person rigorously searched under my ownssinspection.”ss“You might have spared yourself this trouble,” said Dupin. “D——,ssI presume, is not altogether a fool, and, if not, must havessanticipated these waylayings, as a matter of course.”ss“Not altogether a fool,” said G., “but then he’s a poet, which Isstake to be only one remove from a fool.”ss“True,” said Dupin, after a long and thoughtful whiff from hisssmeerschaum, “although I have been guilty of certain doggrelssmyself.”ss“Suppose you detail,” said I, “the particulars of your search.”ss“Why the fact is, we took our time, and we searched _everywhere_.ssI have had long experience in these affairs. I took the entiressbuilding, room by room; devoting the nights of a whole week tosseach. We examined, first, the furniture of each apartment. Wessopened every possible drawer; and I presume you know that, to assproperly trained police agent, such a thing as a secret drawer isssimpossible. Any man is a dolt who permits a ‘secret’ drawer tossescape him in a search of this kind. The thing is so plain. Theressis a certain amount of bulk—of space—to be accounted for in everysscabinet. Then we have accurate rules. The fiftieth part of a linesscould not escape us. After the cabinets we took the chairs. Thesscushions we probed with the fine long needles you have seen messemploy. From the tables we removed the tops.”ss“Why so?”ss“Sometimes the top of a table, or other similarly arranged piecessof furniture, is removed by the person wishing to conceal anssarticle; then the leg is excavated, the article deposited withinssthe cavity, and the top replaced. The bottoms and tops ofssbedposts are employed in the same way.”ss“But could not the cavity be detected by sounding?” I asked.ss“By no means, if, when the article is deposited, a sufficientsswadding of cotton be placed around it. Besides, in our case, wesswere obliged to proceed without noise.”ss“But you could not have removed—you could not have taken tosspieces all articles of furniture in which it would have beensspossible to make a deposit in the manner you mention. A letterssmay be compressed into a thin spiral roll, not differing much inssshape or bulk from a large knitting-needle, and in this form itssmight be inserted into the rung of a chair, for example. You didssnot take to pieces all the chairs?”ss“Certainly not; but we did better—we examined the rungs of everysschair in the hotel, and, indeed the jointings of everyssdescription of furniture, by the aid of a most powerfulssmicroscope. Had there been any traces of recent disturbance wessshould not have failed to detect it instantly. A single grain ofssgimlet-dust, for example, would have been as obvious as an apple.ssAny disorder in the glueing—any unusual gaping in thessjoints—would have sufficed to insure detection.”ss“I presume you looked to the mirrors, between the boards and thessplates, and you probed the beds and the bed-clothes, as well asssthe curtains and carpets.”ss“That of course; and when we had absolutely completed everyssparticle of the furniture in this way, then we examined the housessitself. We divided its entire surface into compartments, which wessnumbered, so that none might be missed; then we scrutinized eachssindividual square inch throughout the premises, including the twosshouses immediately adjoining, with the microscope, as before.”ss“The two houses adjoining!” I exclaimed; “you must have had assgreat deal of trouble.”ss“We had; but the reward offered is prodigious!”ss“You include the grounds about the houses?”ss“All the grounds are paved with brick. They gave us comparativelysslittle trouble. We examined the moss between the bricks, andssfound it undisturbed.”ss“You looked among D——‘s papers, of course, and into the books ofssthe library?”ss“Certainly; we opened every package and parcel; we not onlyssopened every book, but we turned over every leaf in each volume,ssnot contenting ourselves with a mere shake, according to thessfashion of some of our police officers. We also measured thessthickness of every book-cover, with the most accuratessadmeasurement, and applied to each the most jealous scrutiny ofssthe microscope. Had any of the bindings been recently meddledsswith, it would have been utterly impossible that the fact shouldsshave escaped observation. Some five or six volumes, just from thesshands of the binder, we carefully probed, longitudinally, withssthe needles.”ss“You explored the floors beneath the carpets?”ss“Beyond doubt. We removed every carpet, and examined the boardssswith the microscope.”ss“And the paper on the walls?”ss“Yes.”ss“You looked into the cellars?”ss“We did.”ss“Then,” I said, “you have been making a miscalculation, and thessletter is not upon the premises, as you suppose.”ss“I fear you are right there,” said the Prefect. “And now, Dupin,sswhat would you advise me to do?”ss“To make a thorough re-search of the premises.”ss“That is absolutely needless,” replied G——. “I am not more suressthat I breathe than I am that the letter is not at the Hotel.”ss“I have no better advice to give you,” said Dupin. “You have, ofsscourse, an accurate description of the letter?”ss“Oh yes!”—And here the Prefect, producing a memorandum-book,ssproceeded to read aloud a minute account of the internal, andssespecially of the external appearance of the missing document.ssSoon after finishing the perusal of this description, he took hisssdeparture, more entirely depressed in spirits than I had everssknown the good gentleman before.ssIn about a month afterwards he paid us another visit, and foundssus occupied very nearly as before. He took a pipe and a chair andssentered into some ordinary conversation. At length I said,—ss“Well, but G——, what of the purloined letter? I presume you havessat last made up your mind that there is no such thing asssoverreaching the Minister?”ss“Confound him, say I—yes; I made the re-examination, however, asssDupin suggested—but it was all labor lost, as I knew it wouldssbe.”ss“How much was the reward offered, did you say?” asked Dupin.ss“Why, a very great deal—a very liberal reward—I don’t like to saysshow much, precisely; but one thing I will say, that I wouldn’tssmind giving my individual check for fifty thousand francs to anyssone who could obtain me that letter. The fact is, it is becomingssof more and more importance every day; and the reward has beensslately doubled. If it were trebled, however, I could do no moressthan I have done.”ss“Why, yes,” said Dupin, drawlingly, between the whiffs of hisssmeerschaum, “I really—think, G——, you have not exertedssyourself—to the utmost in this matter. You might—do a littlessmore, I think, eh?”ss“How?—in what way?”ss“Why—puff, puff—you might—puff, puff—employ counsel in thessmatter, eh?—puff, puff, puff. Do you remember the story they tellssof Abernethy?”ss“No; hang Abernethy!”ss“To be sure! hang him and welcome. But, once upon a time, asscertain rich miser conceived the design of spunging upon thisssAbernethy for a medical opinion. Getting up, for this purpose, anssordinary conversation in a private company, he insinuated hissscase to the physician, as that of an imaginary individual.ss“‘We will suppose,’ said the miser, ‘that his symptoms are suchssand such; now, doctor, what would you have directed him to take?’ss“‘Take!’ said Abernethy, ‘why, take advice, to be sure.’”ss“But,” said the Prefect, a little discomposed, “I am perfectlysswilling to take advice, and to pay for it. I would really givessfifty thousand francs to any one who would aid me in the matter.”ss“In that case,” replied Dupin, opening a drawer, and producing asscheck-book, “you may as well fill me up a check for the amountssmentioned. When you have signed it, I will hand you the letter.”ssI was astounded. The Prefect appeared absolutelyssthunder-stricken. For some minutes he remained speechless andssmotionless, looking incredulously at my friend with open mouth,ssand eyes that seemed starting from their sockets; then,ssapparently recovering himself in some measure, he seized a pen,ssand after several pauses and vacant stares, finally filled up andsssigned a check for fifty thousand francs, and handed it acrossssthe table to Dupin. The latter examined it carefully andssdeposited it in his pocket-book; then, unlocking an escritoire,sstook thence a letter and gave it to the Prefect. They are persevering, ingenious, cunning, and thoroughlyssversed in the knowledge which their duties seem chiefly tossdemand. Thus, when G—— detailed to us his mode of searching thesspremises at the Hotel D——, I felt entire confidence in his havingssmade a satisfactory investigation—so far as his labors extended.”ss“So far as his labors extended?” said I.ss“Yes,” said Dupin. “The measures adopted were not only the bestssof their kind, but carried out to absolute perfection. Had thessletter been deposited within the range of their search, thesessfellows would, beyond a question, have found it.”ssI merely laughed—but he seemed quite serious in all that he said.ss“The measures, then,” he continued, “were good in their kind, andsswell executed; their defect lay in their being inapplicable tossthe case, and to the man. A certain set of highly ingeniousssresources are, with the Prefect, a sort of Procrustean bed, tosswhich he forcibly adapts his designs. But he perpetually errs byssbeing too deep or too shallow for the matter in hand; and many assschoolboy is a better reasoner than he. I knew one about eightssyears of age, whose success at guessing in the game of ‘even andssodd’ attracted universal admiration. This game is simple, and isssplayed with marbles. One player holds in his hand a number ofssthese toys, and demands of another whether that number is even orssodd. If the guess is right, the guesser wins one; if wrong, hessloses one. The boy to whom I allude won all the marbles of thessschool. Of course he had some principle of guessing; and this layssin mere observation and admeasurement of the astuteness of hisssopponents. For example, an arrant simpleton is his opponent, and,ssholding up his closed hand, asks, ‘are they even or odd?’ Ourssschoolboy replies, ‘odd,’ and loses; but upon the second trial hesswins, for he then says to himself, ‘the simpleton had them evenssupon the first trial, and his amount of cunning is justsssufficient to make him have them odd upon the second; I willsstherefore guess odd;’—he guesses odd, and wins. Now, with asssimpleton a degree above the first, he would have reasoned thus:ss‘This fellow finds that in the first instance I guessed odd, and,ssin the second, he will propose to himself, upon the firstssimpulse, a simple variation from even to odd, as did the firstsssimpleton; but then a second thought will suggest that this issstoo simple a variation, and finally he will decide upon puttingssit even as before. I will therefore guess even;’—he guesses even,ssand wins. Now this mode of reasoning in the schoolboy, whom hisssfellows termed ‘lucky,’—what, in its last analysis, is it?”ss“It is merely,” I said, “an identification of the reasoner’sssintellect with that of his opponent.”ss“It is,” said Dupin; “and, upon inquiring of the boy by whatssmeans he effected the thorough identification in which hissssuccess consisted, I received answer as follows: ‘When I wish tossfind out how wise, or how stupid, or how good, or how wicked isssany one, or what are his thoughts at the moment, I fashion thessexpression of my face, as accurately as possible, in accordancesswith the expression of his, and then wait to see what thoughts orsssentiments arise in my mind or heart, as if to match orsscorrespond with the expression.’ This response of the schoolboysslies at the bottom of all the spurious profundity which has beenssattributed to Rochefoucault, to La Bougive, to Machiavelli, andssto Campanella.”ss“And the identification,” I said, “of the reasoner’s intellectsswith that of his opponent, depends, if I understand you aright,ssupon the accuracy with which the opponent’s intellect isssadmeasured.”ss“For its practical value it depends upon this,” replied Dupin;ss“and the Prefect and his cohort fail so frequently, first, byssdefault of this identification, and, secondly, byssill-admeasurement, or rather through non-admeasurement, of thessintellect with which they are engaged. They consider only theirssown ideas of ingenuity; and, in searching for anything hidden,ssadvert only to the modes in which they would have hidden it. Theyssare right in this much—that their own ingenuity is a faithfulssrepresentative of that of the mass; but when the cunning of thessindividual felon is diverse in character from their own, thessfelon foils them, of course. This always happens when it is abovesstheir own, and very usually when it is below. They have nossvariation of principle in their investigations; at best, whenssurged by some unusual emergency—by some extraordinary reward—theyssextend or exaggerate their old modes of practice, withoutsstouching their principles. What, for example, in this case ofssD——, has been done to vary the principle of action? What is allssthis boring, and probing, and sounding, and scrutinizing with thessmicroscope and dividing the surface of the building intossregistered square inches—what is it all but an exaggeration ofssthe application of the one principle or set of principles ofsssearch, which are based upon the one set of notions regardingsshuman ingenuity, to which the Prefect, in the long routine of hisssduty, has been accustomed? Do you not see he has taken it forssgranted that all men proceed to conceal a letter,—not exactly inssa gimlet hole bored in a chair-leg—but, at least, in somessout-of-the-way hole or corner suggested by the same tenor ofssthought which would urge a man to secrete a letter in assgimlet-hole bored in a chair-leg? And do you not see also, thatsssuch recherchés nooks for concealment are adapted only forssordinary occasions, and would be adopted only by ordinaryssintellects; for, in all cases of concealment, a disposal of thessarticle concealed—a disposal of it in this recherché manner,—is,ssin the very first instance, presumable and presumed; and thus itsssdiscovery depends, not at all upon the acumen, but altogetherssupon the mere care, patience, and determination of the seekers;ssand where the case is of importance—or, what amounts to the samessthing in the political eyes, when the reward is of magnitude,—thessqualities in question have never been known to fail. You will nowssunderstand what I meant in suggesting that, had the purloinedssletter been hidden any where within the limits of the Prefect’sssexamination—in other words, had the principle of its concealmentssbeen comprehended within the principles of the Prefect—itsssdiscovery would have been a matter altogether beyond question.ssThis functionary, however, has been thoroughly mystified; and thessremote source of his defeat lies in the supposition that thessMinister is a fool, because he has acquired renown as a poet. Allssfools are poets; this the Prefect feels; and he is merely guiltyssof a non distributio medii in thence inferring that all poets aressfools.”ss“But is this really the poet?” I asked. “There are two brothers,ssI know; and both have attained reputation in letters. ThessMinister I believe has written learnedly on the DifferentialssCalculus. He is a mathematician, and no poet.”ss“You are mistaken; I know him well; he is both. As poet andssmathematician, he would reason well; as mere mathematician, hesscould not have reasoned at all, and thus would have been at thessmercy of the Prefect.”ss“You surprise me,” I said, “by these opinions, which have beensscontradicted by the voice of the world. You do not mean to set atssnaught the well-digested idea of centuries. The mathematicalssreason has long been regarded as the reason par excellence.”ss“‘Il y a à parièr,’” replied Dupin, quoting from Chamfort, “‘quesstoute idée publique, toute convention reçue est une sottise, carsselle a convenue au plus grand nombre.’ The mathematicians, Issgrant you, have done their best to promulgate the popular errorssto which you allude, and which is none the less an error for itssspromulgation as truth. With an art worthy a better cause, forssexample, they have insinuated the term ‘analysis’ intossapplication to algebra. The French are the originators of thisssparticular deception; but if a term is of any importance—if wordsssderive any value from applicability—then ‘analysis’ conveysss‘algebra’ about as much as, in Latin, ‘ambitus’ impliesss‘ambition,’ ‘_religio_’ ‘religion,’ or ‘_homines honesti_’ a setssof _honorable_ men.”ss“You have a quarrel on hand, I see,” said I, “with some of thessalgebraists of Paris; but proceed.”ss“I dispute the availability, and thus the value, of that reasonsswhich is cultivated in any especial form other than thessabstractly logical. I dispute, in particular, the reason educedssby mathematical study. The mathematics are the science of formssand quantity; mathematical reasoning is merely logic applied tossobservation upon form and quantity. The great error lies insssupposing that even the truths of what is called pure algebra,ssare abstract or general truths. And this error is so egregiousssthat I am confounded at the universality with which it has beenssreceived. Mathematical axioms are not axioms of general truth.ssWhat is true of relation—of form and quantity—is often grosslyssfalse in regard to morals, for example. In this latter science itssis very usually untrue that the aggregated parts are equal to thesswhole. In chemistry also the axiom fails. In the consideration ofssmotive it fails; for two motives, each of a given value, havessnot, necessarily, a value when united, equal to the sum of theirssvalues apart. There are numerous other mathematical truths whichssare only truths within the limits of relation. But thessmathematician argues, from his finite truths, through habit, asssif they were of an absolutely general applicability—as the worldssindeed imagines them to be. Bryant, in his very learnedss‘Mythology,’ mentions an analogous source of error, when he saysssthat ‘although the Pagan fables are not believed, yet we forgetssourselves continually, and make inferences from them as existingssrealities.’ With the algebraists, however, who are Pagansssthemselves, the ‘Pagan fables’ are believed, and the inferencesssare made, not so much through lapse of memory, as through anssunaccountable addling of the brains. In short, I never yetssencountered the mere mathematician who could be trusted out ofssequal roots, or one who did not clandestinely hold it as a pointssof his faith that x2+px was absolutely and unconditionally equalssto q. Say to one of these gentlemen, by way of experiment, if youssplease, that you believe occasions may occur where x2+px is notssaltogether equal to q, and, having made him understand what youssmean, get out of his reach as speedily as convenient, for, beyondssdoubt, he will endeavor to knock you down.ss“I mean to say,” continued Dupin, while I merely laughed at hissslast observations, “that if the Minister had been no more than assmathematician, the Prefect would have been under no necessity ofssgiving me this check. I know him, however, as both mathematicianssand poet, and my measures were adapted to his capacity, withssreference to the circumstances by which he was surrounded. I knewsshim as a courtier, too, and as a bold intriguant. Such a man, Issconsidered, could not fail to be aware of the ordinary policialssmodes of action. He could not have failed to anticipate—andssevents have proved that he did not fail to anticipate—thesswaylayings to which he was subjected. He must have foreseen, Issreflected, the secret investigations of his premises. Hisssfrequent absences from home at night, which were hailed by thessPrefect as certain aids to his success, I regarded only as ruses,ssto afford opportunity for thorough search to the police, and thusssthe sooner to impress them with the conviction to which G——, inssfact, did finally arrive—the conviction that the letter was notssupon the premises. I felt, also, that the whole train of thought,sswhich I was at some pains in detailing to you just now,ssconcerning the invariable principle of policial action insssearches for articles concealed—I felt that this whole train ofssthought would necessarily pass through the mind of the Minister.ssIt would imperatively lead him to despise all the ordinary nooksssof concealment. He could not, I reflected, be so weak as not tosssee that the most intricate and remote recess of his hotel wouldssbe as open as his commonest closets to the eyes, to the probes,ssto the gimlets, and to the microscopes of the Prefect. I saw, inssfine, that he would be driven, as a matter of course, tosssimplicity, if not deliberately induced to it as a matter ofsschoice. The principle of the vis inertiæ, for example, seemsssto be identical in physics and metaphysics. It is not more truessin the former, that a large body is with more difficulty set inssmotion than a smaller one, and that its subsequent momentum issscommensurate with this difficulty, than it is, in the latter,ssthat intellects of the vaster capacity, while more forcible, moressconstant, and more eventful in their movements than those ofssinferior grade, are yet the less readily moved, and moressembarrassed and full of hesitation in the first few steps ofsstheir progress. One party playing requires another to find a given word—thessname of town, river, state or empire—any word, in short, upon thessmotley and perplexed surface of the chart. A novice in the gamessgenerally seeks to embarrass his opponents by giving them thessmost minutely lettered names; but the adept selects such words asssstretch, in large characters, from one end of the chart to thessother. He is well acquaintedsswith my MS., and I just copied into the middle of the blank sheetssthe words—ss“‘— — Un dessein si funeste,ssS’il n’est digne d’Atrée, est digne de Thyeste.ssThey are to be found in Crébillon’s ‘Atrée.’”ssTHE THOUSAND-AND-SECOND TALE OF SCHEHERAZADEssTruth is stranger than fiction.—_Old Saying_ssHaving had occasion, lately, in the course of some Orientalssinvestigations, to consult the Tellmenow Isitsöornot, a worksswhich (like the Zohar of Simeon Jochaides) is scarcely known atssall, even in Europe; and that thess_dénouement_ there given, if not altogether inaccurate, as far asssit goes, is at least to blame in not having gone very muchssfarther.ssFor full information on this interesting topic, I must refer thessinquisitive reader to the “Isitsöornot” itself; but in thessmeantime, I shall be pardoned for giving a summary of what Issthere discovered.ssIt will be remembered, that, in the usual version of the tales, asscertain monarch having good cause to be jealous of his queen, notssonly puts her to death, but makes a vow, by his beard and thessprophet, to espouse each night the most beautiful maiden in hisssdominions, and the next morning to deliver her up to thessexecutioner.ssHaving fulfilled this vow for many years to the letter, and withssa religious punctuality and method that conferred great creditssupon him as a man of devout feeling and excellent sense, he wasssinterrupted one afternoon (no doubt at his prayers) by a visitssfrom his grand vizier, to whose daughter, it appears, there hadssoccurred an idea.ssHer name was Scheherazade, and her idea was, that she wouldsseither redeem the land from the depopulating tax upon its beauty,ssor perish, after the approved fashion of all heroines, in thessattempt.ssAccordingly, and although we do not find it to be leap-yearss(which makes the sacrifice more meritorious), she deputes herssfather, the grand vizier, to make an offer to the king of hersshand. This hand the king eagerly accepts—(he had intended to takessit at all events, and had put off the matter from day to day,ssonly through fear of the vizier),—but, in accepting it now, hessgives all parties very distinctly to understand, that, grandssvizier or no grand vizier, he has not the slightest design ofssgiving up one iota of his vow or of his privileges. When,sstherefore, the fair Scheherazade insisted upon marrying the king,ssand did actually marry him despite her father’s excellent advicessnot to do any thing of the kind—when she would and did marry him,ssI say, will I, nill I, it was with her beautiful black eyes asssthoroughly open as the nature of the case would allow.ssIt seems, however, that this politic damsel (who had been readingssMachiavelli, beyond doubt), had a very ingenious little plot inssher mind. On the night of the wedding, she contrived, upon Issforget what specious pretence, to have her sister occupy a couchsssufficiently near that of the royal pair to admit of easyssconversation from bed to bed; and, a little before cock-crowing,ssshe took care to awaken the good monarch, her husband (who boressher none the worse will because he intended to wring her neck onssthe morrow),—she managed to awaken him, I say, (although onssaccount of a capital conscience and an easy digestion, he sleptsswell) by the profound interest of a story (about a rat and assblack cat, I think) which she was narrating (all in an undertone,ssof course) to her sister. When the day broke, it so happened thatssthis history was not altogether finished, and that Scheherazade,ssin the nature of things could not finish it just then, since itsswas high time for her to get up and be bowstrung—a thing verysslittle more pleasant than hanging, only a trifle more genteel!ssThe king’s curiosity, however, prevailing, I am sorry to say,sseven over his sound religious principles, induced him for thisssonce to postpone the fulfilment of his vow until next morning,ssfor the purpose and with the hope of hearing that night how itssfared in the end with the black cat (a black cat, I think it was)ssand the rat.ssThe night having arrived, however, the lady Scheherazade not onlyssput the finishing stroke to the black cat and the rat (the ratsswas blue) but before she well knew what she was about, foundssherself deep in the intricacies of a narration, having referencess(if I am not altogether mistaken) to a pink horse (with greensswings) that went, in a violent manner, by clockwork, and wassswound up with an indigo key. Through it theressmeandered a glorious river for several thousands of miles. Thisssriver was of unspeakable depth, and of a transparency richer thanssthat of amber. (*10)ssThere were others that sprang from the substance of otherssvegetables; (*11) others that derived their substance from thessbodies of living animals; (*12) and then again, there were othersssthat glowed all over with intense fire; (*13) others that movedssfrom place to place at pleasure, (*14) and what was still moresswonderful, we discovered flowers that lived and breathed andssmoved their limbs at will and had, moreover, the detestablesspassion of mankind for enslaving other creatures, and confiningssthem in horrid and solitary prisons until the fulfillment ofssappointed tasks.’” (*15)ss“Pshaw!” said the king.ss“‘Quitting this land, we soon arrived at another in which thessbees and the birds are mathematicians of such genius andsserudition, that they give daily instructions in the science ofssgeometry to the wise men of the empire. This terrible fowl had no head that we could perceive,ssbut was fashioned entirely of belly, which was of a prodigiousssfatness and roundness, of a soft-looking substance, smooth,ssshining and striped with various colors. In its talons, thessmonster was bearing away to his eyrie in the heavens, a housessfrom which it had knocked off the roof, and in the interior ofsswhich we distinctly saw human beings, who, beyond doubt, were inssa state of frightful despair at the horrible fate which awaitedssthem. This elevated the man-animal very much in myssesteem, and in fact, I now began to feel ashamed of thesscontemptuous familiarity with which I had treated him; for Issfound that the man-animals in general were a nation of the mostsspowerful magicians, who lived with worms in their brain, (*19)sswhich, no doubt, served to stimulate them by their painfulsswrithings and wrigglings to the most miraculous efforts ofssimagination!’”ss“Nonsense!” said the king.ss“‘Among the magicians, were domesticated several animals of verysssingular kinds; for example, there was a huge horse whose bonessswere iron and whose blood was boiling water. (*22)ssAnother of these magi constructed (of like material) a creaturessthat put to shame even the genius of him who made it; for sossgreat were its reasoning powers that, in a second, it performedsscalculations of so vast an extent that they would have requiredssthe united labor of fifty thousand fleshy men for a year. (*24) Another hadssthe faculty of converting the common metals into gold, withoutsseven looking at them during the process. (*25) Another had such assdelicacy of touch that he made a wire so fine as to be invisible.ss(*26) Another had such quickness of perception that he countedssall the separate motions of an elastic body, while it wasssspringing backward and forward at the rate of nine hundredssmillions of times in a second.’” (*27)ss“Absurd!” said the king.ss“‘Another of these magicians, by means of a fluid that nobodyssever yet saw, could make the corpses of his friends brandishsstheir arms, kick out their legs, fight, or even get up and dancessat his will. (*28) Another had cultivated his voice to so greatssan extent that he could have made himself heard from one end ofssthe world to the other. (*29) Another had so long an arm that hesscould sit down in Damascus and indite a letter at Bagdad—orssindeed at any distance whatsoever. —_Joseph Glanville_.ssWe had now reached the summit of the loftiest crag. For somessminutes the old man seemed too much exhausted to speak.ss“Not long ago,” said he at length, “and I could have guided yousson this route as well as the youngest of my sons; but, aboutssthree years past, there happened to me an event such as neversshappened to mortal man—or at least such as no man ever survivedssto tell of—and the six hours of deadly terror which I thenssendured have broken me up body and soul. Nothing would have tempted me to within half a dozen yards ofssits brink.  story with the spot just under your eye.”ss“We are now,” he continued, in that particularizing manner whichssdistinguished him—“we are now close upon the Norwegian coast—inssthe sixty-eighth degree of latitude—in the great province ofssNordland—and in the dreary district of Lofoden. The mountain uponsswhose top we sit is Helseggen, the Cloudy. A panoramassmore deplorably desolate no human imagination can conceive. Tossthe right and left, as far as the eye could reach, there layssoutstretched, like ramparts of the world, lines of horridly blackssand beetling cliff, whose character of gloom was but the moressforcibly illustrated by the surf which reared high up against itssswhite and ghastly crest, howling and shrieking forever. Justssopposite the promontory upon whose apex we were placed, and at assdistance of some five or six miles out at sea, there was visiblessa small, bleak-looking island; or, more properly, its positionsswas discernible through the wilderness of surge in which it wasssenveloped. That a mile tossthe northward is Ambaaren. Yonder are Islesen, Hotholm,ssKeildhelm, Suarven, and Buckholm. Farther off—between Moskoe andssVurrgh—are Otterholm, Flimen, Sandflesen, and Stockholm. Do you see any change in thesswater?”ssWe had now been about ten minutes upon the top of Helseggen, tosswhich we had ascended from the interior of Lofoden, so that wesshad caught no glimpse of the sea until it had burst upon us fromssthe summit. Even while I gazed, this current acquired assmonstrous velocity. Each moment added to its speed—to itsssheadlong impetuosity. In five minutes the whole sea, as far asssVurrgh, was lashed into ungovernable fury; but it was betweenssMoskoe and the coast that the main uproar held its sway. Here thessvast bed of the waters, seamed and scarred into a thousandssconflicting channels, burst suddenly into phrensiedssconvulsion—heaving, boiling, hissing—gyrating in gigantic andssinnumerable vortices, and all whirling and plunging on to thesseastward with a rapidity which water never elsewhere assumesssexcept in precipitous descents.ssIn a few minutes more, there came over the scene another radicalssalteration. Presently oursslittle boat gave herself a shake, just as a dog does in comingssout of the water, and thus rid herself, in some measure, of thessseas. With the wind that nowssdrove us on, we were bound for the whirl of the Ström, andssnothing could save us!ss“You perceive that in crossing the Ström _channel_, we alwayssswent a long way up above the whirl, even in the calmest weather,ssand then had to wait and watch carefully for the slack—but now wesswere driving right upon the pool itself, and in such a hurricanessas this! ‘To be sure,’ I thought, ‘we shall get there just aboutssthe slack—there is some little hope in that’—but in the nextssmoment I cursed myself for being so great a fool as to dream ofsshope at all. Presently he shooksshis head, looking as pale as death, and held up one of hisssfingers, as if to say _‘listen! ‘_ss“At first I could not make out what he meant—but soon a hideousssthought flashed upon me. We were behind the time of the slack, andssthe whirl of the Ström was in full fury!_ss“When a boat is well built, properly trimmed, and not deep laden,ssthe waves in a strong gale, when she is going large, seem alwaysssto slip from beneath her—which appears very strange to asslandsman—and this is what is called _riding_, in sea phrase.ss“Well, so far we had ridden the swells very cleverly; butsspresently a gigantic sea happened to take us right under thesscounter, and bore us with it as it rose—up—up—as if into the sky.ssI would not have believed that any wave could rise so high. Andssthen down we came with a sweep, a slide, and a plunge, that madessme feel sick and dizzy, as if I was falling from some loftyssmountain-top in a dream. If you have never been at sea in assheavy gale, you can form no idea of the confusion of mindssoccasioned by the wind and spray together. They blind, deafen,ssand strangle you, and take away all power of action orssreflection. Since my escape, I have had several conversations on thissssubject with an old school-master of the district; and it wasssfrom him that I learned the use of the words ‘cylinder’ andss‘sphere.’ He explained to me—although I have forgotten thessexplanation—how what I observed was, in fact, the naturalssconsequence of the forms of the floating fragments—and showed messhow it happened that a cylinder, swimming in a vortex, offeredssmore resistance to its suction, and was drawn in with greaterssdifficulty than an equally bulky body, of any form whatever. (*1)ss“There was one startling circumstance which went a great way inssenforcing these observations, and rendering me anxious to turnssthem to account, and this was that, at every revolution, wesspassed something like a barrel, or else the yard or the mast of assvessel, while many of these things, which had been on our levelsswhen I first opened my eyes upon the wonders of the whirlpool,sswere now high up above us, and seemed to have moved but littlessfrom their original station.ss“I no longer hesitated what to do. I now tellssit to _you_—and I can scarcely expect you to put more faith in itssthan did the merry fishermen of Lofoden.”ssVON KEMPELEN AND HIS DISCOVERYssAfter the very minute and elaborate paper by Arago, to sayssnothing of the summary in ‘Silliman’s Journal,’ with the detailedssstatement just published by Lieutenant Maury, it will not besssupposed, of course, that in offering a few hurried remarks inssreference to Von Kempelen’s discovery, I have any design to lookssat the subject in a scientific point of view. My object issssimply, in the first place, to say a few words of Von Kempelensshimself (with whom, some years ago, I had the honor of a slightsspersonal acquaintance), since every thing which concerns him mustssnecessarily, at this moment, be of interest; and, in the secondssplace, to look in a general way, and speculatively, at thessresults of the discovery.ssIt may be as well, however, to premise the cursory observationssswhich I have to offer, by denying, very decidedly, what seems tossbe a general impression (gleaned, as usual in a case of thissskind, from the newspapers), viz.: that this discovery, astoundingssas it unquestionably is, is unanticipated.ssBy reference to the ‘Diary of Sir Humphrey Davy’ (Cottle andssMunroe, London, pp. 150), it will be seen at pp. 53 and 82, thatssthis illustrious chemist had not only conceived the idea now inssquestion, but had actually made no inconsiderable progress,ssexperimentally, in the very identical analysis now sosstriumphantly brought to an issue by Von Kempelen, who although hessmakes not the slightest allusion to it, is, without doubt (I sayssit unhesitatingly, and can prove it, if required), indebted tossthe ‘Diary’ for at least the first hint of his own undertaking.ssThe paragraph from the ‘Courier and Enquirer,’ which is now goingssthe rounds of the press, and which purports to claim thessinvention for a Mr. Kissam, of Brunswick, Maine, appears to me, Issconfess, a little apocryphal, for several reasons; although theressis nothing either impossible or very improbable in the statementssmade. I need not go into details. My opinion of the paragraph isssfounded principally upon its manner. It does not look true.ssPersons who are narrating facts, are seldom so particular as Mr.ssKissam seems to be, about day and date and precise location.ssBesides, if Mr. Kissam actually did come upon the discovery hesssays he did, at the period designated—nearly eight years ago—howsshappens it that he took no steps, on the instant, to reap thessimmense benefits which the merest bumpkin must have known wouldsshave resulted to him individually, if not to the world at large,ssfrom the discovery? It seems to me quite incredible that any manssof common understanding could have discovered what Mr. Kissamsssays he did, and yet have subsequently acted so like a baby—sosslike an owl—as Mr. Kissam admits that he did. By-the-way, who isssMr. Kissam? and is not the whole paragraph in the ‘Courier andssEnquirer’ a fabrication got up to ‘make a talk’? It must bessconfessed that it has an amazingly moon-hoaxy-air. Very littlessdependence is to be placed upon it, in my humble opinion; and ifssI were not well aware, from experience, how very easily men ofssscience are mystified, on points out of their usual range ofssinquiry, I should be profoundly astonished at finding so eminentssa chemist as Professor Draper, discussing Mr. Kissam’s (or is itssMr. Quizzem’s?) pretensions to the discovery, in so serious asstone.ssBut to return to the ‘Diary’ of Sir Humphrey Davy. This pamphletsswas not designed for the public eye, even upon the decease of thesswriter, as any person at all conversant with authorship maysssatisfy himself at once by the slightest inspection of the style.ssAt page 13, for example, near the middle, we read, in referencessto his researches about the protoxide of azote: ‘In less thansshalf a minute the respiration being continued, diminishedssgradually and were succeeded by analogous to gentle pressure onssall the muscles.’ That the respiration was not ‘diminished,’ isssnot only clear by the subsequent context, but by the use of thessplural, ‘were.’ The sentence, no doubt, was thus intended: ‘Inssless than half a minute, the respiration [being continued, thesessfeelings] diminished gradually, and were succeeded by [asssensation] analogous to gentle pressure on all the muscles.’ Asshundred similar instances go to show that the MS. sossinconsiderately published, was merely a rough note-book, meantssonly for the writer’s own eye, but an inspection of the pamphletsswill convince almost any thinking person of the truth of mysssuggestion. The fact is, Sir Humphrey Davy was about the last manssin the world to commit himself on scientific topics. Not only hadsshe a more than ordinary dislike to quackery, but he was morbidlyssafraid of appearing empirical; so that, however fully he mightsshave been convinced that he was on the right track in the matterssnow in question, he would never have spoken out, until he hadssevery thing ready for the most practical demonstration. I verilyssbelieve that his last moments would have been rendered wretched,sscould he have suspected that his wishes in regard to burning thisss‘Diary’ (full of crude speculations) would have been unattendedssto; as, it seems, they were. I say ‘his wishes,’ for that hessmeant to include this note-book among the miscellaneous papersssdirected ‘to be burnt,’ I think there can be no manner of doubt.ssWhether it escaped the flames by good fortune or by bad, yetssremains to be seen. That the passages quoted above, with thessother similar ones referred to, gave Von Kempelen the hint, I dossnot in the slightest degree question; but I repeat, it yetssremains to be seen whether this momentous discovery itselfss(momentous under any circumstances) will be of service orssdisservice to mankind at large. That Von Kempelen and hisssimmediate friends will reap a rich harvest, it would be folly tossdoubt for a moment. They will scarcely be so weak as not toss‘realize,’ in time, by large purchases of houses and land, withssother property of intrinsic value.ssIn the brief account of Von Kempelen which appeared in the ‘HomessJournal,’ and has since been extensively copied, severalssmisapprehensions of the German original seem to have been made byssthe translator, who professes to have taken the passage from asslate number of the Presburg ‘Schnellpost.’ ‘Viele’ has evidentlyssbeen misconceived (as it often is), and what the translatorssrenders by ‘sorrows,’ is probably ‘lieden,’ which, in its truessversion, ‘sufferings,’ would give a totally different complexionssto the whole account; but, of course, much of this is merelyssguess, on my part.ssVon Kempelen, however, is by no means ‘a misanthrope,’ inssappearance, at least, whatever he may be in fact. My acquaintancesswith him was casual altogether; and I am scarcely warranted insssaying that I know him at all; but to have seen and conversedsswith a man of so _prodigious_ a notoriety as he has attained, orss_will_ attain in a few days, is not a small matter, as times go.ss“The Literary World” speaks of him, confidently, as a native ofssPresburg (misled, perhaps, by the account in “The Home Journal”)ssbut I am pleased in being able to state _positively_, since Isshave it from his own lips, that he was born in Utica, in thessState of New York, although both his parents, I believe, are ofssPresburg descent. The family is connected, in some way, withssMäelzel, of Automaton-chess-player memory. In person, he is shortssand stout, with large, _fat_, blue eyes, sandy hair and whiskers,ssa wide but pleasing mouth, fine teeth, and I think a Roman nose.ssThere is some defect in one of his feet. His address is frank,ssand his whole manner noticeable for bonhomie. Altogether, hesslooks, speaks, and acts as little like ‘a misanthrope’ as any manssI ever saw. We were fellow-sojourners for a week about six yearsssago, at Earl’s Hotel, in Providence, Rhode Island; and I presumessthat I conversed with him, at various times, for some three orssfour hours altogether. His principal topics were those of thessday; and nothing that fell from him led me to suspect hisssscientific attainments. He left the hotel before me, intending tossgo to New York, and thence to Bremen; it was in the latter cityssthat his great discovery was first made public; or, rather, itsswas there that he was first suspected of having made it. This isssabout all that I personally know of the now immortal VonssKempelen; but I have thought that even these few details wouldsshave interest for the public.ssThere can be little question that most of the marvellous rumorsssafloat about this affair are pure inventions, entitled to aboutssas much credit as the story of Aladdin’s lamp; and yet, in a casessof this kind, as in the case of the discoveries in California, itssis clear that the truth may be stranger than fiction. Thessfollowing anecdote, at least, is so well authenticated, that wessmay receive it implicitly.ssVon Kempelen had never been even tolerably well off during hisssresidence at Bremen; and often, it was well known, he had beenssput to extreme shifts in order to raise trifling sums. When thessgreat excitement occurred about the forgery on the house ofssGutsmuth & Co., suspicion was directed toward Von Kempelen, onssaccount of his having purchased a considerable property inssGasperitch Lane, and his refusing, when questioned, to explainsshow he became possessed of the purchase money. He was at lengthssarrested, but nothing decisive appearing against him, was in thessend set at liberty. The police, however, kept a strict watch uponsshis movements, and thus discovered that he left home frequently,sstaking always the same road, and invariably giving his watchersssthe slip in the neighborhood of that labyrinth of narrow andsscrooked passages known by the flash name of the ‘Dondergat.’ssFinally, by dint of great perseverance, they traced him to assgarret in an old house of seven stories, in an alley calledssFlatzplatz,—and, coming upon him suddenly, found him, as theyssimagined, in the midst of his counterfeiting operations. Hisssagitation is represented as so excessive that the officers hadssnot the slightest doubt of his guilt. After hand-cuffing him,ssthey searched his room, or rather rooms, for it appears hessoccupied all the mansarde.ssOpening into the garret where they caught him, was a closet, tenssfeet by eight, fitted up with some chemical apparatus, of whichssthe object has not yet been ascertained. In one corner of thesscloset was a very small furnace, with a glowing fire in it, andsson the fire a kind of duplicate crucible—two crucibles connectedssby a tube. One of these crucibles was nearly full of lead in assstate of fusion, but not reaching up to the aperture of the tube,sswhich was close to the brim. The other crucible had some liquidssin it, which, as the officers entered, seemed to be furiouslyssdissipating in vapor. They relate that, on finding himself taken,ssKempelen seized the crucibles with both hands (which were encasedssin gloves that afterwards turned out to be asbestic), and threwssthe contents on the tiled floor. It was now that they hand-cuffedsshim; and before proceeding to ransack the premises they searchedsshis person, but nothing unusual was found about him, excepting asspaper parcel, in his coat-pocket, containing what was afterwardssascertained to be a mixture of antimony and some unknownsssubstance, in nearly, but not quite, equal proportions. Allssattempts at analyzing the unknown substance have, so far, failed,ssbut that it will ultimately be analyzed, is not to be doubted.ssPassing out of the closet with their prisoner, the officers wentssthrough a sort of ante-chamber, in which nothing material wasssfound, to the chemist’s sleeping-room. They here rummaged somessdrawers and boxes, but discovered only a few papers, of nossimportance, and some good coin, silver and gold. At length,sslooking under the bed, they saw a large, common hair trunk,sswithout hinges, hasp, or lock, and with the top lying carelesslyssacross the bottom portion. Upon attempting to draw this trunk outssfrom under the bed, they found that, with their united strengthss(there were three of them, all powerful men), they ‘could notssstir it one inch.’ Much astonished at this, one of them crawledssunder the bed, and looking into the trunk, said:ss‘No wonder we couldn’t move it—why it’s full to the brim of oldssbits of brass!’ssPutting his feet, now, against the wall so as to get a goodsspurchase, and pushing with all his force, while his companionssspulled with all theirs, the trunk, with much difficulty, was slidssout from under the bed, and its contents examined. The supposedssbrass with which it was filled was all in small, smooth pieces,ssvarying from the size of a pea to that of a dollar; but thesspieces were irregular in shape, although more or lessssflat-looking, upon the whole, “very much as lead looks whenssthrown upon the ground in a molten state, and there suffered tossgrow cool.” Now, not one of these officers for a moment suspectedssthis metal to be anything _but_ brass. The idea of its beingss_gold_ never entered their brains, of course; how _could_ such asswild fancy have entered it? And their astonishment may be wellssconceived, when the next day it became known, all over Bremen,ssthat the “lot of brass” which they had carted so contemptuouslyssto the police office, without putting themselves to the troublessof pocketing the smallest scrap, was not only gold—real gold—butssgold far finer than any employed in coinage—gold, in fact,ssabsolutely pure, virgin, without the slightest appreciable alloy.ssI need not go over the details of Von Kempelen’s confession (asssfar as it went) and release, for these are familiar to thesspublic. That he has actually realized, in spirit and in effect,ssif not to the letter, the old chimaera of the philosopher’sssstone, no sane person is at liberty to doubt. The opinions ofssArago are, of course, entitled to the greatest consideration; butsshe is by no means infallible; and what he says of bismuth, in hisssreport to the Academy, must be taken _cum grano salis_. Thesssimple truth is, that up to this period all analysis has failed;ssand until Von Kempelen chooses to let us have the key to his ownsspublished enigma, it is more than probable that the matter willssremain, for years, in statu quo. All that as yet can fairly besssaid to be known is, that ‘Pure gold can be made at will, andssvery readily from lead in connection with certain othersssubstances, in kind and in proportions, unknown.’ssSpeculation, of course, is busy as to the immediate and ultimatessresults of this discovery—a discovery which few thinking personssswill hesitate in referring to an increased interest in the matterssof gold generally, by the late developments in California; andssthis reflection brings us inevitably to another—the exceedingssinopportuneness of Von Kempelen’s analysis. If many weressprevented from adventuring to California, by the meressapprehension that gold would so materially diminish in value, onssaccount of its plentifulness in the mines there, as to render thessspeculation of going so far in search of it a doubtful one—whatssimpression will be wrought now, upon the minds of those about tossemigrate, and especially upon the minds of those actually in thessmineral region, by the announcement of this astounding discoveryssof Von Kempelen? a discovery which declares, in so many words,ssthat beyond its intrinsic worth for manufacturing purposesss(whatever that worth may be), gold now is, or at least soon willssbe (for it cannot be supposed that Von Kempelen can long retainsshis secret), of no greater value than lead, and of far inferiorssvalue to silver. It is, indeed, exceedingly difficult tossspeculate prospectively upon the consequences of the discovery,ssbut one thing may be positively maintained—that the announcementssof the discovery six months ago would have had material influencessin regard to the settlement of California.ssIn Europe, as yet, the most noticeable results have been a risessof two hundred per cent. in the price of lead, and nearlysstwenty-five per cent. that of silver.ssMESMERIC REVELATIONssWhatever doubt may still envelop the _rationale_ of mesmerism, its
startling _facts_ are now almost universally admitted. Of these latter,
those who doubt, are your mere doubters by profession—an unprofitable
and disreputable tribe. There can be no more absolute waste of time
than the attempt to _prove_, at the present day, that man, by mere
exercise of will, can so impress his fellow, as to cast him into an
abnormal condition, of which the phenomena resemble very closely those
of _death_, or at least resemble them more nearly than they do the
phenomena of any other normal condition within our cognizance; that,
while in this state, the person so impressed employs only with effort,
and then feebly, the external organs of sense, yet perceives, with
keenly refined perception, and through channels supposed unknown,
matters beyond the scope of the physical organs; that, moreover, his
intellectual faculties are wonderfully exalted and invigorated; that
his sympathies with the person so impressing him are profound; and,
finally, that his susceptibility to the impression increases with its
frequency, while, in the same proportion, the peculiar phenomena
elicited are more extended and more _pronounced_.

I say that these—which are the laws of mesmerism in its general
features—it would be supererogation to demonstrate; nor shall I inflict
upon my readers so needless a demonstration; to-day. My purpose at
present is a very different one indeed. I am impelled, even in the
teeth of a world of prejudice, to detail without comment the very
remarkable substance of a colloquy, occurring between a sleep-waker and
myself.

I had been long in the habit of mesmerizing the person in question (Mr.
Vankirk), and the usual acute susceptibility and exaltation of the
mesmeric perception had supervened. For many months he had been
laboring under confirmed phthisis, the more distressing effects of
which had been relieved by my manipulations; and on the night of
Wednesday, the fifteenth instant, I was summoned to his bedside.

The invalid was suffering with acute pain in the region of the heart,
and breathed with great difficulty, having all the ordinary symptoms of
asthma. In spasms such as these he had usually found relief from the
application of mustard to the nervous centres, but to-night this had
been attempted in vain.

As I entered his room he greeted me with a cheerful smile, and although
evidently in much bodily pain, appeared to be, mentally, quite at ease.

“I sent for you to-night,” he said, “not so much to administer to my
bodily ailment, as to satisfy me concerning certain psychal impressions
which, of late, have occasioned me much anxiety and surprise. I need
not tell you how sceptical I have hitherto been on the topic of the
soul’s immortality. I cannot deny that there has always existed, as if
in that very soul which I have been denying, a vague half-sentiment of
its own existence. But this half-sentiment at no time amounted to
conviction. With it my reason had nothing to do. All attempts at
logical inquiry resulted, indeed, in leaving me more sceptical than
before. I had been advised to study Cousin. I studied him in his own
works as well as in those of his European and American echoes. The
‘Charles Elwood’ of Mr. Brownson, for example, was placed in my hands.
I read it with profound attention. Throughout I found it logical, but
the portions which were not _merely_ logical were unhappily the initial
arguments of the disbelieving hero of the book. In his summing up it
seemed evident to me that the reasoner had not even succeeded in
convincing himself. His end had plainly forgotten his beginning, like
the government of Trinculo. In short, I was not long in perceiving that
if man is to be intellectually convinced of his own immortality, he
will never be so convinced by the mere abstractions which have been so
long the fashion of the moralists of England, of France, and of
Germany. Abstractions may amuse and exercise, but take no hold on the
mind. Here upon earth, at least, philosophy, I am persuaded, will
always in vain call upon us to look upon qualities as things. The will
may assent—the soul—the intellect, never.

“I repeat, then, that I only half felt, and never intellectually
believed. But latterly there has been a certain deepening of the
feeling, until it has come so nearly to resemble the acquiescence of
reason, that I find it difficult to distinguish between the two. I am
enabled, too, plainly to trace this effect to the mesmeric influence. I
cannot better explain my meaning than by the hypothesis that the
mesmeric exaltation enables me to perceive a train of ratiocination
which, in my abnormal existence, convinces, but which, in full
accordance with the mesmeric phenomena, does not extend, except through
its _effect_, into my normal condition. In sleep-waking, the reasoning
and its conclusion—the cause and its effect—are present together. In my
natural state, the cause vanishing, the effect only, and perhaps only
partially, remains.

“These considerations have led me to think that some good results might
ensue from a series of well-directed questions propounded to me while
mesmerized. You have often observed the profound self-cognizance
evinced by the sleep-waker—the extensive knowledge he displays upon all
points relating to the mesmeric condition itself; and from this
self-cognizance may be deduced hints for the proper conduct of a
catechism.”

I consented of course to make this experiment.  A few passes threw Mr.
Vankirk into the mesmeric sleep. His breathing became immediately more
easy, and he seemed to suffer no physical uneasiness. The following
conversation then ensued:—V. in the dialogue representing the patient,
and P. myself.ss_P._ Are you asleep?ss_V._ Yes—no; I would rather sleep more soundly.ss_P._ [_After a few more passes._] Do you sleep now?ss_V._ Yes.ss_P._ How do you think your present illness will result?ss_V._ [_After a long hesitation and speaking as if with effort_.]ssI must die.ss_P._ Does the idea of death afflict you?ss_V._ [_Very quickly_.] No—no!ss_P._ Are you pleased with the prospect?ss_V._ If I were awake I should like to die, but now it is nossmatter. The mesmeric condition is so near death as to content me.ss_P._ I wish you would explain yourself, Mr. Vankirk.ss_V._ I am willing to do so, but it requires more effort than Issfeel able to make. You do not question me properly.ss_P._ What then shall I ask?ss_V._ You must begin at the beginning.ss_P._ The beginning! But where is the beginning?ss_V._ You know that the beginning is GOD. [_This was said in asslow, fluctuating tone, and with every sign of the most profoundssveneration_.]ss_P._ What then, is God?ss_V._ [_Hesitating for many minutes._] I cannot tell.ss_P._ Is not God spirit?ss_V._ While I was awake I knew what you meant by “spirit,” but nowssit seems only a word—such, for instance, as truth, beauty—assquality, I mean.ss_P._ Is not God immaterial?ss_V._ There is no immateriality—it is a mere word. That which isssnot matter, is not at all—unless qualities are things.ss_P._ Is God, then, material?ss_V._ No. [_This reply startled me very much._]ss_P._ What, then, is he?ss_V._ [_After a long pause, and mutteringly._] I see—but it is assthing difficult to tell. [_Another long pause._] He is notssspirit, for he exists. Nor is he matter, as _you understand it_.ssBut there are _gradations_ of matter of which man knows nothing;ssthe grosser impelling the finer, the finer pervading the grosser.ssThe atmosphere, for example, impels the electric principle, whilessthe electric principle permeates the atmosphere. These gradationsssof matter increase in rarity or fineness, until we arrive at assmatter _unparticled_—without particles—indivisible—_one;_ andsshere the law of impulsion and permeation is modified. Thessultimate, or unparticled matter, not only permeates all thingsssbut impels all things; and thus _is_ all things within itself.ssThis matter is God. What men attempt to embody in the wordss“thought,” is this matter in motion.ss_P._ The metaphysicians maintain that all action is reducible tossmotion and thinking, and that the latter is the origin of thessformer.ss_V._ Yes; and I now see the confusion of idea. Motion is thessaction of _mind_, not of _thinking_. The unparticled matter, orssGod, in quiescence, is (as nearly as we can conceive it) what mensscall mind. And the power of self-movement (equivalent in effectssto human volition) is, in the unparticled matter, the result ofssits unity and omniprevalence; _how_ I know not, and now clearlysssee that I shall never know. But the unparticled matter, set inssmotion by a law, or quality, existing within itself, is thinking.ss_P._ Can you give me no more precise idea of what you term thessunparticled matter?ss_V._ The matters of which man is cognizant escape the senses inssgradation. We have, for example, a metal, a piece of wood, a dropssof water, the atmosphere, a gas, caloric, electricity, thessluminiferous ether. Now we call all these things matter, andssembrace all matter in one general definition; but in spite ofssthis, there can be no two ideas more essentially distinct thanssthat which we attach to a metal, and that which we attach to thessluminiferous ether. When we reach the latter, we feel an almostssirresistible inclination to class it with spirit, or withssnihility. The only consideration which restrains us is ourssconception of its atomic constitution; and here, even, we have tossseek aid from our notion of an atom, as something possessing inssinfinite minuteness, solidity, palpability, weight. Destroy thessidea of the atomic constitution and we should no longer be ablessto regard the ether as an entity, or at least as matter. For wantssof a better word we might term it spirit. Take, now, a stepssbeyond the luminiferous ether—conceive a matter as much more raressthan the ether, as this ether is more rare than the metal, and wessarrive at once (in spite of all the school dogmas) at a uniquessmass—an unparticled matter. For although we may admit infinitesslittleness in the atoms themselves, the infinitude of littlenessssin the spaces between them is an absurdity. There will be asspoint—there will be a degree of rarity, at which, if the atomsssare sufficiently numerous, the interspaces must vanish, and thessmass absolutely coalesce. But the consideration of the atomicssconstitution being now taken away, the nature of the massssinevitably glides into what we conceive of spirit. It is clear,sshowever, that it is as fully matter as before. The truth is, itssis impossible to conceive spirit, since it is impossible tossimagine what is not. When we flatter ourselves that we havessformed its conception, we have merely deceived our understandingssby the consideration of infinitely rarified matter.ss_P._ There seems to me an insurmountable objection to the idea ofssabsolute coalescence;—and that is the very slight resistancessexperienced by the heavenly bodies in their revolutions throughssspace—a resistance now ascertained, it is true, to exist inss_some_ degree, but which is, nevertheless, so slight as to havessbeen quite overlooked by the sagacity even of Newton. We knowssthat the resistance of bodies is, chiefly, in proportion to theirssdensity. Absolute coalescence is absolute density. Where theressare no interspaces, there can be no yielding. An ether,ssabsolutely dense, would put an infinitely more effectual stop tossthe progress of a star than would an ether of adamant or of iron.ss_V._ Your objection is answered with an ease which is nearly inssthe ratio of its apparent unanswerability.—As regards thessprogress of the star, it can make no difference whether the starsspasses through the ether _or the ether through it_. There is nossastronomical error more unaccountable than that which reconcilesssthe known retardation of the comets with the idea of theirsspassage through an ether: for, however rare this ether besssupposed, it would put a stop to all sidereal revolution in assvery far briefer period than has been admitted by thosessastronomers who have endeavored to slur over a point which theyssfound it impossible to comprehend. The retardation actuallyssexperienced is, on the other hand, about that which might bessexpected from the _friction_ of the ether in the instantaneoussspassage through the orb. In the one case, the retarding force isssmomentary and complete within itself—in the other it is endlesslyssaccumulative.ss_P._ But in all this—in this identification of mere matter withssGod—is there nothing of irreverence? [_I was forced to repeatssthis question before the sleep-waker fully comprehended myssmeaning_.]ss_V._ Can you say _why_ matter should be less reverenced thanssmind? But you forget that the matter of which I speak is, in allssrespects, the very “mind” or “spirit” of the schools, so far asssregards its high capacities, and is, moreover, the “matter” ofssthese schools at the same time. God, with all the powersssattributed to spirit, is but the perfection of matter.ss_P._ You assert, then, that the unparticled matter, in motion, isssthought?ss_V._ In general, this motion is the universal thought of thessuniversal mind. This thought creates. All created things are butssthe thoughts of God.ss_P._ You say, “in general.”ss_V._ Yes. The universal mind is God. For new individualities,ss_matter_ is necessary.ss_P._ But you now speak of “mind” and “matter” as do thessmetaphysicians.ss_V._ Yes—to avoid confusion. When I say “mind,” I mean thessunparticled or ultimate matter; by “matter,” I intend all else.ss_P._ You were saying that “for new individualities matter isssnecessary.”ss_V._ Yes; for mind, existing unincorporate, is merely God. Tosscreate individual, thinking beings, it was necessary to incarnatessportions of the divine mind. Thus man is individualized. Divestedssof corporate investiture, he were God. Now, the particular motionssof the incarnated portions of the unparticled matter is thessthought of man; as the motion of the whole is that of God.ss_P._ You say that divested of the body man will be God?ss_V._ [_After much hesitation._] I could not have said this; it isssan absurdity.ss_P._ [_Referring to my notes._] You _did_ say that “divested ofsscorporate investiture man were God.”ss_V._ And this is true.
