But he can never be thus divested—at leastssnever _will be_—else we must imagine an action of God returningssupon itself—a purposeless and futile action. Man is a creature.ssCreatures are thoughts of God. It is the nature of thought to bessirrevocable.ss_P._ I do not comprehend. You say that man will never put off thessbody?ss_V._ I say that he will never be bodiless.ss_P._ Explain.ss_V._ There are two bodies—the rudimental and the complete;sscorresponding with the two conditions of the worm and thessbutterfly. What we call “death,” is but the painfulssmetamorphosis. Our present incarnation is progressive,sspreparatory, temporary. Our future is perfected, ultimate,ssimmortal. The ultimate life is the full design.ss_P._ But of the worm’s metamorphosis we are palpably cognizant.ss_V._ _We_, certainly—but not the worm. The matter of which ourssrudimental body is composed, is within the ken of the organs ofssthat body; or, more distinctly, our rudimental organs are adaptedssto the matter of which is formed the rudimental body; but not tossthat of which the ultimate is composed. The ultimate body thusssescapes our rudimental senses, and we perceive only the shellsswhich falls, in decaying, from the inner form; not that innerssform itself; but this inner form, as well as the shell, isssappreciable by those who have already acquired the ultimate life.ss_P._ You have often said that the mesmeric state very nearlyssresembles death. How is this?ss_V._ When I say that it resembles death, I mean that it resemblesssthe ultimate life; for when I am entranced the senses of myssrudimental life are in abeyance, and I perceive external thingsssdirectly, without organs, through a medium which I shall employssin the ultimate, unorganized life.ss_P._ Unorganized?ss_V._ Yes; organs are contrivances by which the individual isssbrought into sensible relation with particular classes and formsssof matter, to the exclusion of other classes and forms. Thessorgans of man are adapted to his rudimental condition, and tossthat only; his ultimate condition, being unorganized, is ofssunlimited comprehension in all points but one—the nature of thessvolition of God—that is to say, the motion of the unparticledssmatter. You will have a distinct idea of the ultimate body byssconceiving it to be entire brain. This it is _not_; but assconception of this nature will bring you near a comprehension ofsswhat it _is_. A luminous body imparts vibration to thessluminiferous ether. The vibrations generate similar ones withinssthe retina; these again communicate similar ones to the opticssnerve. The nerve conveys similar ones to the brain; the brain,ssalso, similar ones to the unparticled matter which permeates it.ssThe motion of this latter is thought, of which perception is thessfirst undulation. This is the mode by which the mind of thessrudimental life communicates with the external world; and thisssexternal world is, to the rudimental life, limited, through thessidiosyncrasy of its organs. But in the ultimate, unorganizedsslife, the external world reaches the whole body, (which is of asssubstance having affinity to brain, as I have said,) with nossother intervention than that of an infinitely rarer ether thansseven the luminiferous; and to this ether—in unison with it—thesswhole body vibrates, setting in motion the unparticled mattersswhich permeates it. It is to the absence of idiosyncratic organs,sstherefore, that we must attribute the nearly unlimited perceptionssof the ultimate life. To rudimental beings, organs are the cagesssnecessary to confine them until fledged.ss_P._ You speak of rudimental “beings.” Are there other rudimentalssthinking beings than man?ss_V._ The multitudinous conglomeration of rare matter into nebulæ,ssplanets, suns, and other bodies which are neither nebulæ, suns,ssnor planets, is for the sole purpose of supplying _pabulum_ forssthe idiosyncrasy of the organs of an infinity of rudimentalssbeings. But for the necessity of the rudimental, prior to thessultimate life, there would have been no bodies such as these.ssEach of these is tenanted by a distinct variety of organic,ssrudimental, thinking creatures. In all, the organs vary with thessfeatures of the place tenanted. At death, or metamorphosis, thesesscreatures, enjoying the ultimate life—immortality—and cognizantssof all secrets but _the one_, act all things and pass everywheressby mere volition:—indwelling, not the stars, which to us seem thesssole palpabilities, and for the accommodation of which we blindlyssdeem space created—but that SPACE itself—that infinity of whichssthe truly substantive vastness swallows up thessstar-shadows—blotting them out as non-entities from thessperception of the angels.ss_P._ You say that “but for the _necessity_ of the rudimentalsslife” there would have been no stars. But why this necessity?ss_V._ In the inorganic life, as well as in the inorganic matterssgenerally, there is nothing to impede the action of one simpless_unique_ law—the Divine Volition. With the view of producingssimpediment, the organic life and matter, (complex, substantial,ssand law-encumbered,) were contrived.ss_P._ But again—why need this impediment have been produced?ss_V._ The result of law inviolate is perfection—right—negativesshappiness. The result of law violate is imperfection, wrong,sspositive pain. Through the impediments afforded by the number,sscomplexity, and substantiality of the laws of organic life andssmatter, the violation of law is rendered, to a certain extent,sspracticable. Thus pain, which in the inorganic life isssimpossible, is possible in the organic.ss_P._ But to what good end is pain thus rendered possible?ss_V._ All things are either good or bad by comparison. Asssufficient analysis will show that pleasure, in all cases, is butssthe contrast of pain. _Positive_ pleasure is a mere idea. To besshappy at any one point we must have suffered at the same. Neverssto suffer would have been never to have been blessed. But it hasssbeen shown that, in the inorganic life, pain cannot be thus thessnecessity for the organic. The pain of the primitive life ofssEarth, is the sole basis of the bliss of the ultimate life inssHeaven.ss_P._ Still, there is one of your expressions which I find itssimpossible to comprehend—“the truly _substantive_ vastness ofssinfinity.”ss_V._ This, probably, is because you have no sufficiently genericssconception of the term “_substance_” itself. We must not regardssit as a quality, but as a sentiment:—it is the perception, inssthinking beings, of the adaptation of matter to theirssorganization. There are many things on the Earth, which would bessnihility to the inhabitants of Venus—many things visible andsstangible in Venus, which we could not be brought to appreciate asssexisting at all. But to the inorganic beings—to the angels—thesswhole of the unparticled matter is substance—that is to say, thesswhole of what we term “space” is to them the truestsssubstantiality;—the stars, meantime, through what we considersstheir materiality, escaping the angelic sense, just in proportionssas the unparticled matter, through what we consider itsssimmateriality, eludes the organic.ssAs the sleep-waker pronounced these latter words, in a feeblesstone, I observed on his countenance a singular expression, whichsssomewhat alarmed me, and induced me to awake him at once. Nosssooner had I done this, than, with a bright smile irradiating allsshis features, he fell back upon his pillow and expired. I noticedssthat in less than a minute afterward his corpse had all the sternssrigidity of stone. His brow was of the coldness of ice. Thus,ssordinarily, should it have appeared, only after long pressuressfrom Azrael’s hand. Had the sleep-waker, indeed, during thesslatter portion of his discourse, been addressing me from out thessregion of the shadows?ssTHE FACTS IN THE CASE OF M. VALDEMARssOf course I shall not pretend to consider it any matter forsswonder, that the extraordinary case of M. Valdemar has excitedssdiscussion. It would have been a miracle had it not—especiallyssunder the circumstances. Through the desire of all partiesssconcerned, to keep the affair from the public, at least for thesspresent, or until we had farther opportunities forssinvestigation—through our endeavors to effect this—a garbled orssexaggerated account made its way into society, and became thesssource of many unpleasant misrepresentations; and, veryssnaturally, of a great deal of disbelief.ssIt is now rendered necessary that I give the facts—as far as Isscomprehend them myself. They are, succinctly, these:ssMy attention, for the last three years, had been repeatedly drawnssto the subject of Mesmerism; and, about nine months ago itssoccurred to me, quite suddenly, that in the series of experimentsssmade hitherto, there had been a very remarkable and mostssunaccountable omission:—no person had as yet been mesmerized inssarticulo mortis. It remained to be seen, first, whether, in suchsscondition, there existed in the patient any susceptibility to thessmagnetic influence; secondly, whether, if any existed, it wasssimpaired or increased by the condition; thirdly, to what extent,ssor for how long a period, the encroachments of Death might bessarrested by the process. There were other points to bessascertained, but these most excited my curiosity—the last inssespecial, from the immensely important character of itsssconsequences.ssIn looking around me for some subject by whose means I might testssthese particulars, I was brought to think of my friend, M. ErnestssValdemar, the well-known compiler of the “Bibliotheca Forensica,”ssand author (under the nom de plume of Issachar Marx) of thessPolish versions of “Wallenstein” and “Gargantua.” M. Valdemar,sswho has resided principally at Harlem, N.Y., since the year 1839,ssis (or was) particularly noticeable for the extreme spareness ofsshis person—his lower limbs much resembling those of JohnssRandolph; and, also, for the whiteness of his whiskers, inssviolent contrast to the blackness of his hair—the latter, inssconsequence, being very generally mistaken for a wig. Hissstemperament was markedly nervous, and rendered him a good subjectssfor mesmeric experiment. On two or three occasions I had put himssto sleep with little difficulty, but was disappointed in otherssresults which his peculiar constitution had naturally led me tossanticipate. His will was at no period positively, or thoroughly,ssunder my control, and in regard to clairvoyance, I couldssaccomplish with him nothing to be relied upon. I alwaysssattributed my failure at these points to the disordered state ofsshis health. For some months previous to my becoming acquaintedsswith him, his physicians had declared him in a confirmedssphthisis. It was his custom, indeed, to speak calmly of hisssapproaching dissolution, as of a matter neither to be avoided norssregretted.ssWhen the ideas to which I have alluded first occurred to me, itsswas of course very natural that I should think of M. Valdemar. Issknew the steady philosophy of the man too well to apprehend anyssscruples from him; and he had no relatives in America who wouldssbe likely to interfere. I spoke to him frankly upon the subject;ssand, to my surprise, his interest seemed vividly excited. I sayssto my surprise, for, although he had always yielded his personssfreely to my experiments, he had never before given me any tokensssof sympathy with what I did. His disease was of that charactersswhich would admit of exact calculation in respect to the epoch ofssits termination in death; and it was finally arranged between usssthat he would send for me about twenty-four hours before thessperiod announced by his physicians as that of his decease.ssIt is now rather more than seven months since I received, from M.ssValdemar himself, the subjoined note:ssMY DEAR P——,ssYou may as well come now. D—— and F—— are agreed that I cannotsshold out beyond to-morrow midnight; and I think they have hit thesstime very nearly.ssVALDEMARssI received this note within half an hour after it was written,ssand in fifteen minutes more I was in the dying man’s chamber. Isshad not seen him for ten days, and was appalled by the fearfulssalteration which the brief interval had wrought in him. His facesswore a leaden hue; the eyes were utterly lustreless; and thessemaciation was so extreme that the skin had been broken throughssby the cheek-bones. His expectoration was excessive. The pulsesswas barely perceptible. He retained, nevertheless, in a veryssremarkable manner, both his mental power and a certain degree ofssphysical strength. He spoke with distinctness—took somesspalliative medicines without aid—and, when I entered the room,sswas occupied in penciling memoranda in a pocket-book. He wassspropped up in the bed by pillows. Doctors D—— and F—— were inssattendance.ssAfter pressing Valdemar’s hand, I took these gentlemen aside, andssobtained from them a minute account of the patient’s condition.ssThe left lung had been for eighteen months in a semi-osseous orsscartilaginous state, and was, of course, entirely useless for allsspurposes of vitality. Upon thessbed, before that whole company, there lay a nearly liquid mass ofssloathsome—of detestable putrescence.ssTHE BLACK CAT.ssFor the most wild, yet most homely narrative which I am about tosspen, I neither expect nor solicit belief. Mad indeed would I bessto expect it, in a case where my very senses reject their ownssevidence. Yet, mad am I not—and very surely do I not dream. Butssto-morrow I die, and to-day I would unburthen my soul. Myssimmediate purpose is to place before the world, plainly,sssuccinctly, and without comment, a series of mere householdssevents. In their consequences, these events have terrified—havesstortured—have destroyed me. Yet I will not attempt to expoundssthem. To me, they have presented little but horror—to many theysswill seem less terrible than _barroques_. Hereafter, perhaps,sssome intellect may be found which will reduce my phantasm to thesscommon-place—some intellect more calm, more logical, and far lessssexcitable than my own, which will perceive, in the circumstancesssI detail with awe, nothing more than an ordinary succession ofssvery natural causes and effects.ssFrom my infancy I was noted for the docility and humanity of myssdisposition. My tenderness of heart was even so conspicuous as tossmake me the jest of my companions. I was especially fond ofssanimals, and was indulged by my parents with a great variety ofsspets. With these I spent most of my time, and never was so happyssas when feeding and caressing them. This peculiarity of characterssgrew with my growth, and in my manhood, I derived from it one ofssmy principal sources of pleasure. To those who have cherished anssaffection for a faithful and sagacious dog, I need hardly be atssthe trouble of explaining the nature or the intensity of thessgratification thus derivable. Observing my partiality for domesticsspets, she lost no opportunity of procuring those of the mostssagreeable kind. Uplifting anssaxe, and forgetting, in my wrath, the childish dread which hadsshitherto stayed my hand, I aimed a blow at the animal which, ofsscourse, would have proved instantly fatal had it descended as Isswished. Issknew that I could not remove it from the house, either by day orssby night, without the risk of being observed by the neighbors.ssMany projects entered my mind. At one period I thought of cuttingssthe corpse into minute fragments, and destroying them by fire. Atssanother, I resolved to dig a grave for it in the floor of thesscellar. Again, I deliberated about casting it in the well in thessyard—about packing it in a box, as if merchandise, with the usualssarrangements, and so getting a porter to take it from the house.ssFinally I hit upon what I considered a far better expedient thansseither of these. I determined to wall it up in the cellar—as thessmonks of the middle ages are recorded to have walled up theirssvictims.ssFor a purpose such as this the cellar was well adapted. Its wallssswere loosely constructed, and had lately been plasteredssthroughout with a rough plaster, which the dampness of thessatmosphere had prevented from hardening. Moreover, in one of thesswalls was a projection, caused by a false chimney, or fireplace,ssthat had been filled up, and made to resemble the red of thesscellar. I made no doubt that I could readily displace the bricksssat this point, insert the corpse, and wall the whole up asssbefore, so that no eye could detect any thing suspicious. And inssthis calculation I was not deceived. By means of a crow-bar Isseasily dislodged the bricks, and, having carefully deposited thessbody against the inner wall, I propped it in that position,sswhile, with little trouble, I re-laid the whole structure as itssoriginally stood. Having procured mortar, sand, and hair, withssevery possible precaution, I prepared a plaster which could notssbe distinguished from the old, and with this I very carefullysswent over the new brickwork. When I had finished, I feltsssatisfied that all was right. The wall did not present thessslightest appearance of having been disturbed. The rubbish on thessfloor was picked up with the minutest care. I looked aroundsstriumphantly, and said to myself: “Here at least, then, my laborsshas not been in vain.”ssMy next step was to look for the beast which had been the causessof so much wretchedness; for I had, at length, firmly resolved tossput it to death. Had I been able to meet with it, at the moment,ssthere could have been no doubt of its fate; but it appeared thatssthe crafty animal had been alarmed at the violence of my previousssanger, and forebore to present itself in my present mood. It isssimpossible to describe, or to imagine, the deep, the blissfulsssense of relief which the absence of the detested creaturessoccasioned in my bosom. It did not make its appearance during thessnight; and thus for one night at least, since its introductionssinto the house, I soundly and tranquilly slept; aye, slept evensswith the burden of murder upon my soul!ssThe second and the third day passed, and still my tormentor camessnot. Once again I breathed as a freeman. The monster, in terror,sshad fled the premises forever! I should behold it no more! Mysshappiness was supreme! The guilt of my dark deed disturbed me butsslittle. Some few inquiries had been made, but these had beenssreadily answered. Even a search had been instituted—but of coursessnothing was to be discovered. I looked upon my future felicity assssecured.ssUpon the fourth day of the assassination, a party of the policesscame, very unexpectedly, into the house, and proceeded again tossmake rigorous investigation of the premises. Secure, however, inssthe inscrutability of my place of concealment, I felt nossembarrassment whatever. The officers bade me accompany them insstheir search. They left no nook or corner unexplored. At length,ssfor the third or fourth time, they descended into the cellar. Issquivered not in a muscle. My heart beat calmly as that of one whossslumbers in innocence. I walked the cellar from end to end. Issfolded my arms upon my bosom, and roamed easily to and fro. Thesspolice were thoroughly satisfied and prepared to depart. The gleessat my heart was too strong to be restrained. I burned to say ifssbut one word, by way of triumph, and to render doubly sure theirssassurance of my guiltlessness.ss“Gentlemen,” I said at last, as the party ascended the steps, “Issdelight to have allayed your suspicions. I wish you all health,ssand a little more courtesy. By the bye, gentlemen, this—this is assvery well-constructed house.” (In the rabid desire to saysssomething easily, I scarcely knew what I uttered at all.)—“I maysssay an _excellently_ well-constructed house. These walls—are youssgoing, gentlemen?—these walls are solidly put together;” andsshere, through the mere phrenzy of bravado, I rapped heavily, withssa cane which I held in my hand, upon that very portion of thessbrick-work behind which stood the corpse of the wife of my bosom.ssBut may God shield and deliver me from the fangs of thessArch-Fiend! No sooner had the reverberation of my blows sunk intosssilence, than I was answered by a voice from within the tomb!—byssa cry, at first muffled and broken, like the sobbing of a child,ssand then quickly swelling into one long, loud, and continuousssscream, utterly anomalous and inhuman—a howl—a wailing shriek,sshalf of horror and half of triumph, such as might have arisenssonly out of hell, conjointly from the throats of the damned insstheir agony and of the demons that exult in the damnation.ssOf my own thoughts it is folly to speak. Swooning, I staggered tossthe opposite wall. For one instant the party upon the stairsssremained motionless, through extremity of terror and of awe. Inssthe next, a dozen stout arms were toiling at the wall. It fellssbodily. The corpse, already greatly decayed and clotted withssgore, stood erect before the eyes of the spectators. Upon itssshead, with red extended mouth and solitary eye of fire, sat thesshideous beast whose craft had seduced me into murder, and whosessinforming voice had consigned me to the hangman. I had walled thessmonster up within the tomb!ssTHE FALL OF THE HOUSE OF USHERssSon cœur est un luth suspendu;ssSitôt qu’on le touche il résonne..

—_De Béranger_.ssDuring the whole of a dull, dark, and soundless day in the autumnssof the year, when the clouds hung oppressively low in thessheavens, I had been passing alone, on horseback, through asssingularly dreary tract of country; and at length found myself,ssas the shades of the evening drew on, within view of thessmelancholy House of Usher. I know not how it was—but, with thessfirst glimpse of the building, a sense of insufferable gloomsspervaded my spirit. I say insufferable; for the feeling wasssunrelieved by any of that half-pleasurable, because poetic,sssentiment, with which the mind usually receives even the sternestssnatural images of the desolate or terrible. I looked upon thessscene before me—upon the mere house, and the simple landscapessfeatures of the domain—upon the bleak walls—upon the vacantsseye-like windows—upon a few rank sedges—and upon a few whitesstrunks of decayed trees—with an utter depression of soul which Isscan compare to no earthly sensation more properly than to thessafter-dream of the reveller upon opium—the bitter lapse intosseveryday life—the hideous dropping off of the veil. There was anssiciness, a sinking, a sickening of the heart—an unredeemedssdreariness of thought which no goading of the imagination couldsstorture into aught of the sublime. What was it—I paused tossthink—what was it that so unnerved me in the contemplation of thessHouse of Usher? It was a mystery all insoluble; nor could Issgrapple with the shadowy fancies that crowded upon me as Isspondered. I was forced to fall back upon the unsatisfactoryssconclusion, that while, beyond doubt, there _are_ combinations ofssvery simple natural objects which have the power of thusssaffecting us, still the analysis of this power lies amongssconsiderations beyond our depth. It was possible, I reflected,ssthat a mere different arrangement of the particulars of thessscene, of the details of the picture, would be sufficient tossmodify, or perhaps to annihilate its capacity for sorrowfulssimpression; and, acting upon this idea, I reined my horse to thessprecipitous brink of a black and lurid tarn that lay in unruffledsslustre by the dwelling, and gazed down—but with a shudder evenssmore thrilling than before—upon the remodelled and invertedssimages of the gray sedge, and the ghastly tree-stems, and thessvacant and eye-like windows.ssNevertheless, in this mansion of gloom I now proposed to myself asssojourn of some weeks. Its proprietor, Roderick Usher, had beenssone of my boon companions in boyhood; but many years had elapsedsssince our last meeting. A letter, however, had lately reached messin a distant part of the country—a letter from him—which, in itssswildly importunate nature, had admitted of no other than asspersonal reply. The MS. gave evidence of nervous agitation. Thesswriter spoke of acute bodily illness—of a mental disorder whichssoppressed him—and of an earnest desire to see me, as his best,ssand indeed his only personal friend, with a view of attempting,ssby the cheerfulness of my society, some alleviation of hisssmalady. It was the manner in which all this, and much more, wassssaid—it was the apparent _heart_ that went with his request—whichssallowed me no room for hesitation; and I accordingly obeyedssforthwith what I still considered a very singular summons.ssAlthough, as boys, we had been even intimate associates, yet Issreally knew little of my friend. His reserve had been alwaysssexcessive and habitual. I was aware, however, that his veryssancient family had been noted, time out of mind, for a peculiarsssensibility of temperament, displaying itself, through long ages,ssin many works of exalted art, and manifested, of late, inssrepeated deeds of munificent yet unobtrusive charity, as well asssin a passionate devotion to the intricacies, perhaps even moressthan to the orthodox and easily recognisable beauties, of musicalssscience. I had learned, too, the very remarkable fact, that thessstem of the Usher race, all time-honored as it was, had putssforth, at no period, any enduring branch; in other words, thatssthe entire family lay in the direct line of descent, and hadssalways, with very trifling and very temporary variation, so lain.ssIt was this deficiency, I considered, while running over inssthought the perfect keeping of the character of the premises withssthe accredited character of the people, and while speculatingssupon the possible influence which the one, in the long lapse ofsscenturies, might have exercised upon the other—it was thisssdeficiency, perhaps, of collateral issue, and the consequentssundeviating transmission, from sire to son, of the patrimony withssthe name, which had, at length, so identified the two as to mergessthe original title of the estate in the quaint and equivocalssappellation of the “House of Usher”—an appellation which seemedssto include, in the minds of the peasantry who used it, both thessfamily and the family mansion.ssI have said that the sole effect of my somewhat childishssexperiment—that of looking down within the tarn—had been tossdeepen the first singular impression. There can be no doubt thatssthe consciousness of the rapid increase of my superstition—forsswhy should I not so term it?—served mainly to accelerate thessincrease itself. Such, I have long known, is the paradoxical lawssof all sentiments having terror as a basis. And it might havessbeen for this reason only, that, when I again uplifted my eyes tossthe house itself, from its image in the pool, there grew in myssmind a strange fancy—a fancy so ridiculous, indeed, that I butssmention it to show the vivid force of the sensations whichssoppressed me. I had so worked upon my imagination as really tossbelieve that about the whole mansion and domain there hung anssatmosphere peculiar to themselves and their immediate vicinity—anssatmosphere which had no affinity with the air of heaven, butsswhich had reeked up from the decayed trees, and the gray wall,ssand the silent tarn—a pestilent and mystic vapor, dull, sluggish,ssfaintly discernible, and leaden-hued.ssShaking off from my spirit what _must_ have been a dream, Issscanned more narrowly the real aspect of the building. Itsssprincipal feature seemed to be that of an excessive antiquity.ssThe discoloration of ages had been great. Minute fungi overspreadssthe whole exterior, hanging in a fine tangled web-work from thesseaves. Yet all this was apart from any extraordinaryssdilapidation. No portion of the masonry had fallen; and theressappeared to be a wild inconsistency between its still perfectssadaptation of parts, and the crumbling condition of thessindividual stones. In this there was much that reminded me of thessspecious totality of old wood-work which has rotted for longssyears in some neglected vault, with no disturbance from thessbreath of the external air. Beyond this indication of extensivessdecay, however, the fabric gave little token of instability.ssPerhaps the eye of a scrutinizing observer might have discoveredssa barely perceptible fissure, which, extending from the roof ofssthe building in front, made its way down the wall in a zigzagssdirection, until it became lost in the sullen waters of the tarn.ssNoticing these things, I rode over a short causeway to the house.ssA servant in waiting took my horse, and I entered the Gothicssarchway of the hall. While the objectsssaround me—while the carvings of the ceilings, the sombresstapestries of the walls, the ebon blackness of the floors, andssthe phantasmagoric armorial trophies which rattled as I strode,sswere but matters to which, or to such as which, I had beenssaccustomed from my infancy—while I hesitated not to acknowledgesshow familiar was all this—I still wondered to find how unfamiliarsswere the fancies which ordinary images were stirring up. Dark draperies hung upon thesswalls. The general furniture was profuse, comfortless, antique,ssand tattered. Many books and musical instruments lay scatteredssabout, but failed to give any vitality to the scene. I felt thatssI breathed an atmosphere of sorrow. An air of stern, deep, andssirredeemable gloom hung over and pervaded all.ssUpon my entrance, Usher arose from a sofa on which he had beensslying at full length, and greeted me with a vivacious warmthsswhich had much in it, I at first thought, of an overdonesscordiality—of the constrained effort of the _ennuyé_ man of thessworld. Surely, man had never before so terribly altered, in sossbrief a period, as had Roderick Usher! It was with difficultyssthat I could bring myself to admit the identity of the wan beingssbefore me with the companion of my early boyhood. Yet thesscharacter of his face had been at all times remarkable. Asscadaverousness of complexion; an eye large, liquid, and luminousssbeyond comparison; lips somewhat thin and very pallid, but of asssurpassingly beautiful curve; a nose of a delicate Hebrew model,ssbut with a breadth of nostril unusual in similar formations; assfinely moulded chin, speaking, in its want of prominence, of asswant of moral energy; hair of a more than web-like softness andsstenuity; these features, with an inordinate expansion above thessregions of the temple, made up altogether a countenance notsseasily to be forgotten. And now in the mere exaggeration of thessprevailing character of these features, and of the expressionssthey were wont to convey, lay so much of change that I doubted tosswhom I spoke. The now ghastly pallor of the skin, and the nowssmiraculous lustre of the eye, above all things startled and evenssawed me. The silken hair, too, had been suffered to grow allssunheeded, and as, in its wild gossamer texture, it floated ratherssthan fell about the face, I could not, even with effort, connectssits Arabesque expression with any idea of simple humanity.ssIn the manner of my friend I was at once struck with anssincoherence—an inconsistency; and I soon found this to arise fromssa series of feeble and futile struggles to overcome an habitualsstrepidancy—an excessive nervous agitation. For something of thisssnature I had indeed been prepared, no less by his letter, than byssreminiscences of certain boyish traits, and by conclusionsssdeduced from his peculiar physical conformation and temperament.ssHis action was alternately vivacious and sullen. His voice variedssrapidly from a tremulous indecision (when the animal spiritsssseemed utterly in abeyance) to that species of energeticssconcision—that abrupt, weighty, unhurried, and hollow-soundingssenunciation—that leaden, self-balanced and perfectly modulatedssguttural utterance, which may be observed in the lost drunkard,ssor the irreclaimable eater of opium, during the periods of hisssmost intense excitement.ssIt was thus that he spoke of the object of my visit, of hisssearnest desire to see me, and of the solace he expected me tossafford him. A sensation ofssstupor oppressed me, as my eyes followed her retreating steps.ssWhen a door, at length, closed upon her, my glance soughtssinstinctively and eagerly the countenance of the brother—but hesshad buried his face in his hands, and I could only perceive thatssa far more than ordinary wanness had overspread the emaciatedssfingers through which trickled many passionate tears.ssThe disease of the lady Madeline had long baffled the skill ofssher physicians. The verses, whichsswere entitled “The Haunted Palace,” ran very nearly, if notssaccurately, thus:ssI.ssIn the greenest of our valleys,ssBy good angels tenanted,ssOnce a fair and stately palace—ssRadiant palace—reared its head.ssIn the monarch Thought’s dominion—ssIt stood there!ssNever seraph spread a pinionssOver fabric half so fair.ssII.ssBanners yellow, glorious, golden,ssOn its roof did float and flow;ss(This—all this—was in the oldenssTime long ago)ssAnd every gentle air that dallied,ssIn that sweet day,ssAlong the ramparts plumed and pallid,ssA winged odor went away.ssIII.ssWanderers in that happy valleyssThrough two luminous windows sawssSpirits moving musicallyssTo a lute’s well-tunéd law,ssRound about a throne, where sittingss(Porphyrogene!)ssIn state his glory well befitting,ssThe ruler of the realm was seen.ssIV.ssAnd all with pearl and ruby glowingssWas the fair palace door,ssThrough which came flowing, flowing, flowing,ssAnd sparkling evermore,ssA troop of Echoes whose sweet dutyssWas but to sing,ssIn voices of surpassing beauty,ssThe wit and wisdom of their king.ssV.ssBut evil things, in robes of sorrow,ssAssailed the monarch’s high estate;ss(Ah, let us mourn, for never morrowssShall dawn upon him, desolate!)ssAnd, round about his home, the gloryssThat blushed and bloomedssIs but a dim-remembered storyssOf the old time entombed.ssVI.ssAnd travellers now within that valley,ssThrough the red-litten windows, seessVast forms that move fantasticallyssTo a discordant melody;ssWhile, like a rapid ghastly river,ssThrough the pale door,ssA hideous throng rush out forever,ssAnd laugh—but smile no more.ssI well remember that suggestions arising from this ballad, led usssinto a train of thought wherein there became manifest an opinionssof Usher’s which I mention not so much on account of its novelty,ss(for other men * have thought thus,) as on account of thesspertinacity with which he maintained it. This opinion, in itsssgeneral form, was that of the sentience of all vegetable things.ssBut, in his disordered fancy, the idea had assumed a more daringsscharacter, and trespassed, under certain conditions, upon thesskingdom of inorganization. Such opinions need no comment, and Isswill make none.ss* Watson, Dr. Percival, Spallanzani, and especially the Bishop ofssLandaff.—See “Chemical Essays,” vol v.ssOur books—the books which, for years, had formed no small portionssof the mental existence of the invalid—were, as might besssupposed, in strict keeping with this character of phantasm. The worldly reason,sshowever, assigned for this singular proceeding, was one which Issdid not feel at liberty to dispute. There were delirious fancies such as thessmadman fashions. To and fro inssthe seven chambers there stalked, in fact, a multitude of dreams.ssAnd these—the dreams—writhed in and about, taking hue from thessrooms, and causing the wild music of the orchestra to seem as thessecho of their steps. In thesspure arrogance of the reason, we have all overlooked it. We havesssuffered its existence to escape our senses, solely through wantssof belief—of faith;—whether it be faith in Revelation, or faithssin the Kabbala. The idea of it has never occurred to us, simplyssbecause of its supererogation. We saw no need of the impulse—forssthe propensity. We could not perceive its necessity. We could notssunderstand, that is to say, we could not have understood, had thessnotion of this primum mobile ever obtruded itself;—we could notsshave understood in what manner it might be made to further thessobjects of humanity, either temporal or eternal. It cannot bessdenied that phrenology and, in great measure, allssmetaphysicianism have been concocted a priori. The intellectualssor logical man, rather than the understanding or observant man,ssset himself to imagine designs—to dictate purposes to God. Havingssthus fathomed, to his satisfaction, the intentions of Jehovah,ssout of these intentions he built his innumerable systems of mind.ssIn the matter of phrenology, for example, we first determined,ssnaturally enough, that it was the design of the Deity that manssshould eat. We then assigned to man an organ of alimentiveness,ssand this organ is the scourge with which the Deity compels man,sswill-I nill-I, into eating. Secondly, having settled it to bessGod’s will that man should continue his species, we discovered anssorgan of amativeness, forthwith. And so with combativeness, withssideality, with causality, with constructiveness,—so, in short,sswith every organ, whether representing a propensity, a moralsssentiment, or a faculty of the pure intellect. And in thesessarrangements of the Principia of human action, the Spurzheimites,sswhether right or wrong, in part, or upon the whole, have butssfollowed, in principle, the footsteps of their predecessors;ssdeducing and establishing every thing from the preconceivedssdestiny of man, and upon the ground of the objects of hisssCreator.ssIt would have been wiser, it would have been safer, to classifyss(if classify we must) upon the basis of what man usually orssoccasionally did, and was always occasionally doing, rather thanssupon the basis of what we took it for granted the Deity intendedsshim to do. If we cannot comprehend God in his visible works, howssthen in his inconceivable thoughts, that call the works intossbeing? If we cannot understand him in his objective creatures,sshow then in his substantive moods and phases of creation?ssInduction, _a posteriori_, would have brought phrenology tossadmit, as an innate and primitive principle of human action, assparadoxical something, which we may call _perverseness_, for wantssof a more characteristic term. In the sense I intend, it is, inssfact, a _mobile_ without motive, a motive not _motivirt_. Throughssits promptings we act without comprehensible object; or, if thisssshall be understood as a contradiction in terms, we may so farssmodify the proposition as to say, that through its promptings wessact, for the reason that we should _not_. In theory, no reasonsscan be more unreasonable, but, in fact, there is none moressstrong. With certain minds, under certain conditions, it becomesssabsolutely irresistible. I am not more certain that I breathe,ssthan that the assurance of the wrong or error of any action isssoften the one unconquerable _force_ which impels us, and alonessimpels us to its prosecution. Nor will this overwhelming tendencyssto do wrong for the wrong’s sake, admit of analysis, orssresolution into ulterior elements. It is a radical, a primitivessimpulse—elementary. It will be said, I am aware, that when wesspersist in acts because we feel we should not persist in them,ssour conduct is but a modification of that which ordinarilysssprings from the combativeness of phrenology. But a glance willssshow the fallacy of this idea. The phrenological combativenesssshas for its essence, the necessity of self-defence. It is oursssafeguard against injury. Its principle regards our well-being;ssand thus the desire to be well is excited simultaneously with itsssdevelopment. It follows, that the desire to be well must bessexcited simultaneously with any principle which shall be merely assmodification of combativeness, but in the case of that somethingsswhich I term perverseness, the desire to be well is not only notssaroused, but a strongly antagonistical sentiment exists.ssAn appeal to one’s own heart is, after all, the best reply to thesssophistry just noticed. No one who trustingly consults andssthoroughly questions his own soul, will be disposed to deny thessentire radicalness of the propensity in question. It is not moressincomprehensible than distinctive. There lives no man who at somessperiod has not been tormented, for example, by an earnest desiressto tantalize a listener by circumlocution. The speaker is awaressthat he displeases; he has every intention to please, he isssusually curt, precise, and clear; the most laconic and luminoussslanguage is struggling for utterance upon his tongue; it is onlysswith difficulty that he restrains himself from giving it flow; hessdreads and deprecates the anger of him whom he addresses; yet,ssthe thought strikes him, that by certain involutions andssparentheses this anger may be engendered. That single thought isssenough. The impulse increases to a wish, the wish to a desire,ssthe desire to an uncontrollable longing, and the longing (to thessdeep regret and mortification of the speaker, and in defiance ofssall consequences) is indulged.ssWe have a task before us which must be speedily performed. Wessknow that it will be ruinous to make delay. The most importantsscrisis of our life calls, trumpet-tongued, for immediate energyssand action. We glow, we are consumed with eagerness to commencessthe work, with the anticipation of whose glorious result oursswhole souls are on fire. It must, it shall be undertaken to-day,ssand yet we put it off until to-morrow; and why? There is nossanswer, except that we feel perverse, using the word with nosscomprehension of the principle. To-morrow arrives, and with it assmore impatient anxiety to do our duty, but with this veryssincrease of anxiety arrives, also, a nameless, a positivelyssfearful, because unfathomable, craving for delay. This cravingssgathers strength as the moments fly. The last hour for action isssat hand. We tremble with the violence of the conflict withinssus,—of the definite with the indefinite—of the substance with thessshadow. But, if the contest have proceeded thus far, it is thessshadow which prevails,—we struggle in vain. The clock strikes,ssand is the knell of our welfare. At the same time, it is thesschanticleer-note to the ghost that has so long overawed us. Itssflies—it disappears—we are free. The old energy returns. We willsslabor now. Alas, it is too late!ssWe stand upon the brink of a precipice. We peer into the abyss—wessgrow sick and dizzy. Our first impulse is to shrink from thessdanger. Unaccountably we remain. By slow degrees our sickness andssdizziness and horror become merged in a cloud of unnamablessfeeling. By gradations, still more imperceptible, this cloudssassumes shape, as did the vapor from the bottle out of whichssarose the genius in the Arabian Nights. But out of this our cloudssupon the precipice’s edge, there grows into palpability, a shape,ssfar more terrible than any genius or any demon of a tale, and yetssit is but a thought, although a fearful one, and one which chillsssthe very marrow of our bones with the fierceness of the delightssof its horror. It is merely the idea of what would be oursssensations during the sweeping precipitancy of a fall from such assheight. And this fall—this rushing annihilation—for the veryssreason that it involves that one most ghastly and loathsome ofssall the most ghastly and loathsome images of death and sufferingsswhich have ever presented themselves to our imagination—for thisssvery cause do we now the most vividly desire it. And because ourssreason violently deters us from the brink, therefore do we thessmost impetuously approach it. There is no passion in nature sossdemoniacally impatient, as that of him who, shuddering upon thessedge of a precipice, thus meditates a plunge. To indulge, for assmoment, in any attempt at thought, is to be inevitably lost; forssreflection but urges us to forbear, and therefore it is, I say,ssthat we cannot. If there be no friendly arm to check us, or if wessfail in a sudden effort to prostrate ourselves backward from thessabyss, we plunge, and are destroyed.ssExamine these similar actions as we will, we shall find themssresulting solely from the spirit of the Perverse. We perpetratessthem because we feel that we should not. Beyond or behind thisssthere is no intelligible principle; and we might, indeed, deemssthis perverseness a direct instigation of the Arch-Fiend, were itssnot occasionally known to operate in furtherance of good.ssI have said thus much, that in some measure I may answer yourssquestion—that I may explain to you why I am here—that I mayssassign to you something that shall have at least the faint aspectssof a cause for my wearing these fetters, and for my tenantingssthis cell of the condemned. Had I not been thus prolix, you mightsseither have misunderstood me altogether, or, with the rabble,sshave fancied me mad. As it is, you will easily perceive that I amssone of the many uncounted victims of the Imp of the Perverse.ssIt is impossible that any deed could have been wrought with assmore thorough deliberation. For weeks, for months, I ponderedssupon the means of the murder. I rejected a thousand schemes,ssbecause their accomplishment involved a _chance_ of detection. Atsslength, in reading some French memoirs, I found an account of assnearly fatal illness that occurred to Madame Pilau, through thessagency of a candle accidentally poisoned. The idea struck myssfancy at once. I knew my victim’s habit of reading in bed. Issknew, too, that his apartment was narrow and ill-ventilated. ButssI need not vex you with impertinent details. The next morning he was discovered dead in his bed,ssand the coroner’s verdict was—“Death by the visitation of God.”ssHaving inherited his estate, all went well with me for years. Thessidea of detection never once entered my brain. Of the remains ofssthe fatal taper I had myself carefully disposed. I had left nossshadow of a clew by which it would be possible to convict, orsseven to suspect me of the crime. It is inconceivable how rich asssentiment of satisfaction arose in my bosom as I reflected uponssmy absolute security. For a very long period of time I wasssaccustomed to revel in this sentiment. It afforded me more realssdelight than all the mere worldly advantages accruing from mysssin. But there arrived at length an epoch, from which thesspleasurable feeling grew, by scarcely perceptible gradations,ssinto a haunting and harassing thought. It harassed because itsshaunted. I could scarcely get rid of it for an instant. It isssquite a common thing to be thus annoyed with the ringing in ourssears, or rather in our memories, of the burthen of some ordinarysssong, or some unimpressive snatches from an opera. Nor will we bessthe less tormented if the song in itself be good, or the operassair meritorious. In this manner, at last, I would perpetuallysscatch myself pondering upon my security, and repeating, in a lowssundertone, the phrase, “I am safe.”ssOne day, whilst sauntering along the streets, I arrested myselfssin the act of murmuring, half aloud, these customary syllables.ssIn a fit of petulance, I remodelled them thus: “I am safe—I amsssafe—yes—if I be not fool enough to make open confession!”ssNo sooner had I spoken these words, than I felt an icy chillsscreep to my heart. I had had some experience in these fits ofssperversity (whose nature I have been at some trouble to explain),ssand I remembered well that in no instance I had successfullyssresisted their attacks. And now my own casual self-suggestionssthat I might possibly be fool enough to confess the murder ofsswhich I had been guilty, confronted me, as if the very ghost ofsshim whom I had murdered—and beckoned me on to death.ssAt first, I made an effort to shake off this nightmare of thesssoul. I walked vigorously—faster—still faster—at length I ran. Issfelt a maddening desire to shriek aloud. Every succeeding wave ofssthought overwhelmed me with new terror, for, alas! I well, toosswell understood that to think, in my situation, was to be lost. Issstill quickened my pace. I bounded like a madman through thesscrowded thoroughfares. At length, the populace took the alarm,ssand pursued me. I felt then the consummation of my fate. Could Isshave torn out my tongue, I would have done it, but a rough voicessresounded in my ears—a rougher grasp seized me by the shoulder. Issturned—I gasped for breath. For a moment I experienced all thesspangs of suffocation; I became blind, and deaf, and giddy; andssthen some invisible fiend, I thought, struck me with his broadsspalm upon the back. The long imprisoned secret burst forth fromssmy soul.ssThey say that I spoke with a distinct enunciation, but withssmarked emphasis and passionate hurry, as if in dread ofssinterruption before concluding the brief but pregnant sentencesssthat consigned me to the hangman and to hell.ssHaving related all that was necessary for the fullest judicialssconviction, I fell prostrate in a swoon.ssBut why shall I say more? To-day I wear these chains, and amss_here!_ To-morrow I shall be fetterless!—_but where?_ssTHE ISLAND OF THE FAYssNullus enim locus sine genio est.—_Servius_.ss“La musique,” says Marmontel, in those “Contes Moraux” (*1) whichssin all our translations, we have insisted upon calling “MoralssTales,” as if in mockery of their spirit—“la musique est le seulssdes talents qui jouissent de lui-même; tous les autres veulentssdes temoins.” He here confounds the pleasure derivable from sweetsssounds with the capacity for creating them. No more than anyssother talent, is that for music susceptible of completessenjoyment, where there is no second party to appreciate itsssexercise. And it is only in common with other talents that itssproduces effects which may be fully enjoyed in solitude. The ideasswhich the raconteur has either failed to entertain clearly, orsshas sacrificed in its expression to his national love of point,ssis, doubtless, the very tenable one that the higher order ofssmusic is the most thoroughly estimated when we are exclusivelyssalone. The proposition, in this form, will be admitted at once byssthose who love the lyre for its own sake, and for its spiritualssuses. But there is one pleasure still within the reach of fallenssmortality and perhaps only one—which owes even more than doesssmusic to the accessory sentiment of seclusion. I mean thesshappiness experienced in the contemplation of natural scenery. Insstruth, the man who would behold aright the glory of God uponssearth must in solitude behold that glory. To me, at least, thesspresence—not of human life only, but of life in any other formssthan that of the green things which grow upon the soil and aressvoiceless—is a stain upon the landscape—is at war with the geniusssof the scene. I love, indeed, to regard the dark valleys, and thessgray rocks, and the waters that silently smile, and the forestsssthat sigh in uneasy slumbers, and the proud watchful mountainsssthat look down upon all,—I love to regard these as themselves butssthe colossal members of one vast animate and sentient whole—asswhole whose form (that of the sphere) is the most perfect andssmost inclusive of all; whose path is among associate planets;sswhose meek handmaiden is the moon, whose mediate sovereign is thesssun; whose life is eternity, whose thought is that of a God;sswhose enjoyment is knowledge; whose destinies are lost inssimmensity, whose cognizance of ourselves is akin with our ownsscognizance of the animalculae which infest the brain—a beingsswhich we, in consequence, regard as purely inanimate and materialssmuch in the same manner as these animalculae must thus regard us.ssOur telescopes and our mathematical investigations assure us onssevery hand—notwithstanding the cant of the more ignorant of thesspriesthood—that space, and therefore that bulk, is an importantssconsideration in the eyes of the Almighty. The cycles in whichssthe stars move are those best adapted for the evolution, withoutsscollision, of the greatest possible number of bodies. The formsssof those bodies are accurately such as, within a given surface,ssto include the greatest possible amount of matter;—while thesssurfaces themselves are so disposed as to accommodate a densersspopulation than could be accommodated on the same surfacesssotherwise arranged. Nor is it any argument against bulk being anssobject with God, that space itself is infinite; for there may bessan infinity of matter to fill it. And since we see clearly thatssthe endowment of matter with vitality is a principle—indeed, asssfar as our judgments extend, the leading principle in thessoperations of Deity,—it is scarcely logical to imagine itssconfined to the regions of the minute, where we daily trace it,ssand not extending to those of the august. As we find cycle withinsscycle without end,—yet all revolving around one far-distantsscentre which is the God-head, may we not analogically suppose inssthe same manner, life within life, the less within the greater,ssand all within the Spirit Divine? In short, we are madly erring,ssthrough self-esteem, in believing man, in either his temporal orssfuture destinies, to be of more moment in the universe than thatssvast “clod of the valley” which he tills and contemns, and tosswhich he denies a soul for no more profound reason than that hessdoes not behold it in operation. (*2)ssThese fancies, and such as these, have always given to myssmeditations among the mountains and the forests, by the riversssand the ocean, a tinge of what the everyday world would not failssto term fantastic. My wanderings amid such scenes have been many,ssand far-searching, and often solitary; and the interest withsswhich I have strayed through many a dim, deep valley, or gazedssinto the reflected Heaven of many a bright lake, has been anssinterest greatly deepened by the thought that I have strayed andssgazed alone. What flippant Frenchman was it who said in allusionssto the well-known work of Zimmerman, that, “la solitude est unessbelle chose; mais il faut quelqu’un pour vous dire que lasssolitude est une belle chose?” The epigram cannot be gainsayed;ssbut the necessity is a thing that does not exist.ssIt was during one of my lonely journeyings, amid a far distantssregion of mountain locked within mountain, and sad rivers andssmelancholy tarn writhing or sleeping within all—that I chancedssupon a certain rivulet and island. (_Exequy on the death of his wife, by Henry King, Bishop of
Chichester_.)ssIll-fated and mysterious man!—bewildered in the brilliancy ofssthine own imagination, and fallen in the flames of thine ownssyouth! Again in fancy I behold thee! “There is one painting,” said he, without being aware of my notice of
the tragedy—“there is still one painting which you have not seen.” And

Human art could have done no more in the delineation of her superhuman
beauty. The same ethereal figure which stood before me the preceding

ThessInquisition was in the hands of its enemies.ssTHE PREMATURE BURIALssThere are certain themes of which the interest is all-absorbing,ssbut which are too entirely horrible for the purposes ofsslegitimate fiction. These the mere romanticist must eschew, if hessdo not wish to offend or to disgust. They are with proprietysshandled only when the severity and majesty of Truth sanctify andsssustain them. We thrill, for example, with the most intense ofss“pleasurable pain” over the accounts of the Passage of thessBeresina, of the Earthquake at Lisbon, of the Plague at London,ssof the Massacre of St. Bartholomew, or of the stifling of thesshundred and twenty-three prisoners in the Black Hole at Calcutta.ssBut in these accounts it is the fact——it is the reality——it isssthe history which excites. As inventions, we should regard themsswith simple abhorrence.ssI have mentioned some few of the more prominent and augustsscalamities on record; but in these it is the extent, not lessssthan the character of the calamity, which so vividly impressesssthe fancy. I need not remind the reader that, from the long andssweird catalogue of human miseries, I might have selected manyssindividual instances more replete with essential suffering thanssany of these vast generalities of disaster. The truesswretchedness, indeed—the ultimate woe——is particular, notssdiffuse. That the ghastly extremes of agony are endured by manssthe unit, and never by man the mass——for this let us thank assmerciful God!ssTo be buried while alive is, beyond question, the most terrificssof these extremes which has ever fallen to the lot of meressmortality. That it has frequently, very frequently, so fallensswill scarcely be denied by those who think. The boundaries whichssdivide Life from Death are at best shadowy and vague. Who shallsssay where the one ends, and where the other begins? We know thatssthere are diseases in which occur total cessations of all thessapparent functions of vitality, and yet in which these cessationsssare merely suspensions, properly so called. They are onlysstemporary pauses in the incomprehensible mechanism. A certainssperiod elapses, and some unseen mysterious principle again setsssin motion the magic pinions and the wizard wheels. The silversscord was not for ever loosed, nor the golden bowl irreparablyssbroken. But where, meantime, was the soul?ssApart, however, from the inevitable conclusion, _a priori_ thatsssuch causes must produce such effects——that the well-knownssoccurrence of such cases of suspended animation must naturallyssgive rise, now and then, to premature interments—apart from thisssconsideration, we have the direct testimony of medical andssordinary experience to prove that a vast number of suchssinterments have actually taken place. I might refer at once, ifssnecessary, to a hundred well-authenticated instances. One of veryssremarkable character, and of which the circumstances may be freshssin the memory of some of my readers, occurred, not very long ago,ssin the neighboring city of Baltimore, where it occasioned asspainful, intense, and widely-extended excitement. The wife of onessof the most respectable citizens—a lawyer of eminence and assmember of Congress—was seized with a sudden and unaccountablessillness, which completely baffled the skill of her physicians.ssAfter much suffering she died, or was supposed to die. Thus she remained, and thus she rotted, erect.ssIn the year 1810, a case of living inhumation happened in France,ssattended with circumstances which go far to warrant the assertionssthat truth is, indeed, stranger than fiction. The heroine of thessstory was a Mademoiselle Victorine Lafourcade, a young girl ofssillustrious family, of wealth, and of great personal beauty.ssAmong her numerous suitors was Julien Bossuet, a poorss_litterateur_, or journalist of Paris. When we reflect how veryssrarely, from the nature of the case, we have it in our power tossdetect them, we must admit that they may frequently occur withoutssour cognizance. Scarcely, in truth, is a graveyard everssencroached upon, for any purpose, to any great extent, thatssskeletons are not found in postures which suggest the mostssfearful of suspicions.ssFearful indeed the suspicion—but more fearful the doom! It may bessasserted, without hesitation, that no event is so terribly wellssadapted to inspire the supremeness of bodily and of mentalssdistress, as is burial before death. The unendurable oppressionssof the lungs—the stifling fumes from the damp earth—the clingingssto the death garments—the rigid embrace of the narrow house—thessblackness of the absolute Night—the silence like a sea thatssoverwhelms—the unseen but palpable presence of the ConquerorssWorm—these things, with the thoughts of the air and grass above,sswith memory of dear friends who would fly to save us if butssinformed of our fate, and with consciousness that of this fatessthey can never be informed—that our hopeless portion is that ofssthe really dead—these considerations, I say, carry into thessheart, which still palpitates, a degree of appalling andssintolerable horror from which the most daring imagination mustssrecoil. We know of nothing so agonizing upon Earth—we can dreamssof nothing half so hideous in the realms of the nethermost Hell.ssAnd thus all narratives upon this topic have an interestssprofound; an interest, nevertheless, which, through the sacredssawe of the topic itself, very properly and very peculiarlyssdepends upon our conviction of the truth of the matter narrated.ssWhat I have now to tell is of my own actual knowledge—of my ownsspositive and personal experience.ssFor several years I had been subject to attacks of the singularssdisorder which physicians have agreed to term catalepsy, inssdefault of a more definitive title. Although both the immediatessand the predisposing causes, and even the actual diagnosis, ofssthis disease are still mysterious, its obvious and apparentsscharacter is sufficiently well understood. Its variations seem tossbe chiefly of degree. Sometimes the patient lies, for a day only,ssor even for a shorter period, in a species of exaggeratedsslethargy. He is senseless and externally motionless; but thesspulsation of the heart is still faintly perceptible; some tracesssof warmth remain; a slight color lingers within the centre of thesscheek; and, upon application of a mirror to the lips, we canssdetect a torpid, unequal, and vacillating action of the lungs.ssThen again the duration of the trance is for weeks—even forssmonths; while the closest scrutiny, and the most rigorous medicalsstests, fail to establish any material distinction between thessstate of the sufferer and what we conceive of absolute death.ssVery usually he is saved from premature interment solely by thessknowledge of his friends that he has been previously subject tosscatalepsy, by the consequent suspicion excited, and, above all,ssby the non-appearance of decay. The advances of the malady are,ssluckily, gradual. The first manifestations, although marked, aressunequivocal. The fits grow successively more and moressdistinctive, and endure each for a longer term than thesspreceding. In this lies the principal security from inhumation.ssThe unfortunate whose first attack should be of the extremesscharacter which is occasionally seen, would almost inevitably bessconsigned alive to the tomb.ssMy own case differed in no important particular from thosessmentioned in medical books. Sometimes, without any apparentsscause, I sank, little by little, into a condition ofsssemi-syncope, or half swoon; and, in this condition, withoutsspain, without ability to stir, or, strictly speaking, to think,ssbut with a dull lethargic consciousness of life and of thesspresence of those who surrounded my bed, I remained, until thesscrisis of the disease restored me, suddenly, to perfectsssensation. At other times I was quickly and impetuously smitten.ssI grew sick, and numb, and chilly, and dizzy, and so fellssprostrate at once. Then, for weeks, all was void, and black, andsssilent, and Nothing became the universe. Total annihilation couldssbe no more. Like twinkling stars that sparkle in the evening blue.ss—_Giles Fletcher_.ssFrom his cradle to his grave a gale of prosperity bore my friendssEllison along. Nor do I use the word prosperity in its meressworldly sense. I mean it as synonymous with happiness. The personssof whom I speak seemed born for the purpose of foreshadowing thessdoctrines of Turgot, Price, Priestley, and Condorcet—ofssexemplifying by individual instance what has been deemed thesschimera of the perfectionists. In the brief existence of EllisonssI fancy that I have seen refuted the dogma, that in man’s veryssnature lies some hidden principle, the antagonist of bliss. Anssanxious examination of his career has given me to understand thatssin general, from the violation of a few simple laws of humanityssarises the wretchedness of mankind—that as a species we have inssour possession the as yet unwrought elements of content—and that,sseven now, in the present darkness and madness of all thought onssthe great question of the social condition, it is not impossiblessthat man, the individual, under certain unusual and highlyssfortuitous conditions, may be happy.ssWith opinions such as these my young friend, too, was fullyssimbued, and thus it is worthy of observation that thessuninterrupted enjoyment which distinguished his life was, inssgreat measure, the result of preconcert. It is indeed evidentssthat with less of the instinctive philosophy which, now and then,ssstands so well in the stead of experience, Mr. Ellison would havessfound himself precipitated, by the very extraordinary success ofsshis life, into the common vortex of unhappiness which yawns forssthose of pre-eminent endowments. But it is by no means my objectssto pen an essay on happiness. The ideas of my friend may besssummed up in a few words. He admitted but four elementaryssprinciples, or more strictly, conditions of bliss. That which hessconsidered chief was (strange to say!) the simple and purelyssphysical one of free exercise in the open air. “The health,” hesssaid, “attainable by other means is scarcely worth the name.” Hessinstanced the ecstasies of the fox-hunter, and pointed to thesstillers of the earth, the only people who, as a class, can bessfairly considered happier than others. His second condition wasssthe love of woman. His third, and most difficult of realization,sswas the contempt of ambition. His fourth was an object ofssunceasing pursuit; and he held that, other things being equal,ssthe extent of attainable happiness was in proportion to thessspirituality of this object.ssEllison was remarkable in the continuous profusion of good giftssslavished upon him by fortune. In personal grace and beauty hessexceeded all men. His intellect was of that order to which thessacquisition of knowledge is less a labor than an intuition and assnecessity. His family was one of the most illustrious of thessempire. This gentleman had amassed a princely fortune, and,sshaving no immediate connections, conceived the whim of sufferingsshis wealth to accumulate for a century after his decease.ssMinutely and sagaciously directing the various modes ofssinvestment, he bequeathed the aggregate amount to the nearest ofssblood, bearing the name of Ellison, who should be alive at thessend of the hundred years. Many attempts had been made to setssaside this singular bequest; their ex post facto characterssrendered them abortive; but the attention of a jealous governmentsswas aroused, and a legislative act finally obtained, forbiddingssall similar accumulations. This act, however, did not preventssyoung Ellison from entering into possession, on his twenty-firstssbirthday, as the heir of his ancestor Seabright, of a fortune ofssfour hundred and fifty millions of dollars. (*1)ssWhen it had become known that such was the enormous wealthssinherited, there were, of course, many speculations as to thessmode of its disposal. The magnitude and the immediatessavailability of the sum bewildered all who thought on the topic.ssThe possessor of any appreciable amount of money might have beenssimagined to perform any one of a thousand things. With richesssmerely surpassing those of any citizen, it would have been easyssto suppose him engaging to supreme excess in the fashionablessextravagances of his time—or busying himself with politicalssintrigue—or aiming at ministerial power—or purchasing increase ofssnobility—or collecting large museums of virtu—or playing thessmunificent patron of letters, of science, of art—or endowing, andssbestowing his name upon extensive institutions of charity. Butssfor the inconceivable wealth in the actual possession of thessheir, these objects and all ordinary objects were felt to affordsstoo limited a field. But Ellison maintained that the richest,ssthe truest, and most natural, if not altogether the mostssextensive province, had been unaccountably neglected. Nossdefinition had spoken of the landscape-gardener as of the poet;ssyet it seemed to my friend that the creation of thesslandscape-garden offered to the proper Muse the most magnificentssof opportunities. Nosssuch paradises are to be found in reality as have glowed on thesscanvas of Claude. Who shall presume to imitate thesscolors of the tulip, or to improve the proportions of the lily ofssthe valley? The criticism which says, of sculpture orssportraiture, that here nature is to be exalted or idealizedssrather than imitated, is in error. Having, Isssay, felt its truth here; for the feeling is no affectation orsschimera. The mathematics afford no more absolute demonstrationsssthan the sentiments of his art yields the artist. The original beauty is neverssso great as that which may be introduced. Of course, every thingssdepends on the selection of a spot with capabilities. What issssaid about detecting and bringing into practice nice relations ofsssize, proportion, and color, is one of those mere vaguenesses ofssspeech which serve to veil inaccuracy of thought. The phrasessquoted may mean any thing, or nothing, and guides in no degree.ssThat the true result of the natural style of gardening is seenssrather in the absence of all defects and incongruities than inssthe creation of any special wonders or miracles, is a propositionssbetter suited to the grovelling apprehension of the herd than tossthe fervid dreams of the man of genius. The negative meritsssuggested appertains to that hobbling criticism which, inssletters, would elevate Addison into apotheosis. In truth, whilessthat virtue which consists in the mere avoidance of vice appealsssdirectly to the understanding, and can thus be circumscribed inssrule, the loftier virtue, which flames in creation, can bessapprehended in its results alone. Rule applies but to the meritsssof denial—to the excellencies which refrain. Beyond these, thesscritical art can but suggest. We may be instructed to build ass“Cato,” but we are in vain told how to conceive a Parthenon or anss“Inferno.” The thing done, however; the wonder accomplished; andssthe capacity for apprehension becomes universal. The sophists ofssthe negative school who, through inability to create, havessscoffed at creation, are now found the loudest in applause. What,ssin its chrysalis condition of principle, affronted their demuressreason, never fails, in its maturity of accomplishment, to extortssadmiration from their instinct of beauty.ss“The author’s observations on the artificial style,” continuedssEllison, “are less objectionable. A mixture of pure art in assgarden scene adds to it a great beauty. This is just; as also isssthe reference to the sense of human interest. The principlessexpressed is incontrovertible—but there may be something beyondssit. There may be an object in keeping with the principle—anssobject unattainable by the means ordinarily possessed byssindividuals, yet which, if attained, would lend a charm to thesslandscape-garden far surpassing that which a sense of merelysshuman interest could bestow. A poet, having very unusualsspecuniary resources, might, while retaining the necessary idea ofssart or culture, or, as our author expresses it, of interest, sossimbue his designs at once with extent and novelty of beauty, asssto convey the sentiment of spiritual interference. It will bessseen that, in bringing about such result, he secures all thessadvantages of interest or design, while relieving his work of thessharshness or technicality of the worldly art. In the most ruggedssof wildernesses—in the most savage of the scenes of puressnature—there is apparent the art of a creator; yet this art isssapparent to reflection only; in no respect has it the obviousssforce of a feeling. Now let us suppose this sense of the Almightyssdesign to be one step depressed—to be brought into something likessharmony or consistency with the sense of human art—to form anssintermedium between the two:—let us imagine, for example, asslandscape whose combined vastness and definitiveness—whose unitedssbeauty, magnificence, and strangeness, shall convey the idea ofsscare, or culture, or superintendence, on the part of beingssssuperior, yet akin to humanity—then the sentiment of interest issspreserved, while the art intervolved is made to assume the air ofssan intermediate or secondary nature—a nature which is not God,ssnor an emanation from God, but which still is nature in the sensessof the handiwork of the angels that hover between man and God.”ssIt was in devoting his enormous wealth to the embodiment of assvision such as this—in the free exercise in the open air ensuredssby the personal superintendence of his plans—in the unceasingssobject which these plans afforded—in the high spirituality of thessobject—in the contempt of ambition which it enabled him truly tossfeel—in the perennial springs with which it gratified, withoutsspossibility of satiating, that one master passion of his soul,ssthe thirst for beauty, above all, it was in the sympathy of asswoman, not unwomanly, whose loveliness and love enveloped hisssexistence in the purple atmosphere of Paradise, that Ellisonssthought to find, and found, exemption from the ordinary cares ofsshumanity, with a far greater amount of positive happiness thanssever glowed in the rapt day-dreams of De Staël.ssI despair of conveying to the reader any distinct conception ofssthe marvels which my friend did actually accomplish. I wish tossdescribe, but am disheartened by the difficulty of description,ssand hesitate between detail and generality. Perhaps the bettersscourse will be to unite the two in their extremes.ssMr. Ellison’s first step regarded, of course, the choice of asslocality, and scarcely had he commenced thinking on this point,sswhen the luxuriant nature of the Pacific Islands arrested hisssattention. In fact, he had made up his mind for a voyage to thessSouth Seas, when a night’s reflection induced him to abandon thessidea. “Were I misanthropic,” he said, “such a locale would suitssme. The thoroughness of its insulation and seclusion, and thessdifficulty of ingress and egress, would in such case be the charmssof charms; but as yet I am not Timon. I wish the composure butssnot the depression of solitude. There must remain with me asscertain control over the extent and duration of my repose. Theresswill be frequent hours in which I shall need, too, the sympathyssof the poetic in what I have done. Let me seek, then, a spot notssfar from a populous city—whose vicinity, also, will best enablessme to execute my plans.”ssIn search of a suitable place so situated, Ellison travelled forssseveral years, and I was permitted to accompany him. A thousandssspots with which I was enraptured he rejected without hesitation,ssfor reasons which satisfied me, in the end, that he was right. Wesscame at length to an elevated table-land of wonderful fertilityssand beauty, affording a panoramic prospect very little less inssextent than that of Aetna, and, in Ellison’s opinion as well asssmy own, surpassing the far-famed view from that mountain in allssthe true elements of the picturesque.ss“I am aware,” said the traveller, as he drew a sigh of deepssdelight after gazing on this scene, entranced, for nearly ansshour, “I know that here, in my circumstances, nine-tenths of thessmost fastidious of men would rest content. This panorama isssindeed glorious, and I should rejoice in it but for the excess ofssits glory. The taste of all the architects I have ever knownssleads them, for the sake of ‘prospect,’ to put up buildings onsshill-tops. The error is obvious. Grandeur in any of its moods,ssbut especially in that of extent, startles, excites—and thenssfatigues, depresses. For the occasional scene nothing can bessbetter—for the constant view nothing worse. And, in the constantssview, the most objectionable phase of grandeur is that of extent;ssthe worst phase of extent, that of distance. It is at war withssthe sentiment and with the sense of seclusion—the sentiment andsssense which we seek to humor in ‘retiring to the country.’ Insslooking from the summit of a mountain we cannot help feelingssabroad in the world. The heart-sick avoid distant prospects as asspestilence.”ssIt was not until toward the close of the fourth year of oursssearch that we found a locality with which Ellison professedsshimself satisfied. It is, of course, needless to say where wasssthe locality. The late death of my friend, in causing his domainssto be thrown open to certain classes of visitors, has given tossArnheim a species of secret and subdued if not solemn celebrity,sssimilar in kind, although infinitely superior in degree, to thatsswhich so long distinguished Fonthill.ssThe usual approach to Arnheim was by the river. The visitor leftssthe city in the early morning. During the forenoon he passedssbetween shores of a tranquil and domestic beauty, on which grazedssinnumerable sheep, their white fleeces spotting the vivid greenssof rolling meadows. By degrees the idea of cultivation subsidedssinto that of merely pastoral care. This slowly became merged in asssense of retirement—this again in a consciousness of solitude. Asssthe evening approached, the channel grew more narrow; the banksssmore and more precipitous; and these latter were clothed in rich,ssmore profuse, and more sombre foliage. The water increased insstransparency. The stream took a thousand turns, so that at nossmoment could its gleaming surface be seen for a greater distancessthan a furlong. At every instant the vessel seemed imprisonedsswithin an enchanted circle, having insuperable and impenetrablesswalls of foliage, a roof of ultramarine satin, and no floor—thesskeel balancing itself with admirable nicety on that of a phantomssbark which, by some accident having been turned upside down,ssfloated in constant company with the substantial one, for thesspurpose of sustaining it. The channel now became a gorge—althoughssthe term is somewhat inapplicable, and I employ it merely becausessthe language has no word which better represents the mostssstriking—not the most distinctive—feature of the scene. Thesscharacter of gorge was maintained only in the height andssparallelism of the shores; it was lost altogether in their othersstraits. The walls of the ravine (through which the clear waterssstill tranquilly flowed) arose to an elevation of a hundred andssoccasionally of a hundred and fifty feet, and inclined so muchsstoward each other as, in a great measure, to shut out the lightssof day; while the long plume-like moss which depended denselyssfrom the intertwining shrubberies overhead, gave the whole chasmssan air of funereal gloom. He was,ssmoreover, enwrapt in an exquisite sense of the strange. Notssa dead branch—not a withered leaf—not a stray pebble—not a patchssof the brown earth was anywhere visible.
