It was about two hundred yards in diameter,ssand girt in at all points but one—that immediately fronting thessvessel as it entered—by hills equal in general height to thesswalls of the chasm, although of a thoroughly different character.ssTheir sides sloped from the water’s edge at an angle of somessforty-five degrees, and they were clothed from base to summit—notssa perceptible point escaping—in a drapery of the most gorgeousssflower-blossoms; scarcely a green leaf being visible among thesssea of odorous and fluctuating color. This basin was of greatssdepth, but so transparent was the water that the bottom, whichssseemed to consist of a thick mass of small round alabastersspebbles, was distinctly visible by glimpses—that is to say,sswhenever the eye could permit itself not to see, far down in thessinverted heaven, the duplicate blooming of the hills. On thesesslatter there were no trees, nor even shrubs of any size. Thessimpressions wrought on the observer were those of richness,sswarmth, color, quietude, uniformity, softness, delicacy,ssdaintiness, voluptuousness, and a miraculous extremeness ofssculture that suggested dreams of a new race of fairies,sslaborious, tasteful, magnificent, and fastidious; but as the eyesstraced upward the myriad-tinted slope, from its sharp junctionsswith the water to its vague termination amid the folds ofssoverhanging cloud, it became, indeed, difficult not to fancy asspanoramic cataract of rubies, sapphires, opals, and goldenssonyxes, rolling silently out of the sky.ssThe visitor, shooting suddenly into this bay from out the gloomssof the ravine, is delighted but astounded by the full orb of thessdeclining sun, which he had supposed to be already far below thesshorizon, but which now confronts him, and forms the solesstermination of an otherwise limitless vista seen through anothersschasm-like rift in the hills.ssBut here the voyager quits the vessel which has borne him so far,ssand descends into a light canoe of ivory, stained with arabesquessdevices in vivid scarlet, both within and without. Itsslies on the surface of the bay with the proud grace of a swan. Onssits ermined floor reposes a single feathery paddle of satin-wood;ssbut no oarsmen or attendant is to be seen. The landssundulated very remarkably; and my path, for the last hour, hadsswound about and about so confusedly, in its effort to keep in thessvalleys, that I no longer knew in what direction lay the sweetssvillage of B——, where I had determined to stop for the night. Thesssun had scarcely shone—strictly speaking—during the day, whichssnevertheless, had been unpleasantly warm. A smoky mist,ssresembling that of the Indian summer, enveloped all things, andssof course, added to my uncertainty. Not that I cared much aboutssthe matter. If I did not hit upon the village before sunset, orsseven before dark, it was more than possible that a little Dutchssfarmhouse, or something of that kind, would soon make itsssappearance—although, in fact, the neighborhood (perhaps onssaccount of being more picturesque than fertile) was very sparselyssinhabited. At all events, with my knapsack for a pillow, and mysshound as a sentry, a bivouac in the open air was just the thingsswhich would have amused me. I sauntered on, therefore, quite atssease—Ponto taking charge of my gun—until at length, just as I hadssbegun to consider whether the numerous little glades that ledsshither and thither, were intended to be paths at all, I wasssconducted by one of them into an unquestionable carriage track.ssThere could be no mistaking it. The traces of light wheels weressevident; and although the tall shrubberies and overgrownssundergrowth met overhead, there was no obstruction whateverssbelow, even to the passage of a Virginian mountain wagon—the mostssaspiring vehicle, I take it, of its kind. The road, however,ssexcept in being open through the wood—if wood be not too weightyssa name for such an assemblage of light trees—and except in thessparticulars of evident wheel-tracks—bore no resemblance to anyssroad I had before seen. The tracks of which I speak were butssfaintly perceptible—having been impressed upon the firm, yetsspleasantly moist surface of—what looked more like green Genoesessvelvet than any thing else. It was grass, clearly—but grass suchssas we seldom see out of England—so short, so thick, so even, andssso vivid in color. Not a single impediment lay in thesswheel-route—not even a chip or dead twig. The stones that oncessobstructed the way had been carefully _placed_—not thrown—alongssthe sides of the lane, so as to define its boundaries at bottomsswith a kind of half-precise, half-negligent, and whollysspicturesque definition. Clumps of wild flowers grew everywhere,ssluxuriantly, in the interspaces.ssWhat to make of all this, of course I knew not. Here was artssundoubtedly—that did not surprise me—all roads, in the ordinarysssense, are works of art; nor can I say that there was much tosswonder at in the mere excess of art manifested; all that seemedssto have been done, might have been done here—with such naturalss“capabilities” (as they have it in the books on LandscapessGardening)—with very little labor and expense. No; it was not thessamount but the character of the art which caused me to take assseat on one of the blossomy stones and gaze up and down thisssfairy-like avenue for half an hour or more in bewilderedssadmiration. One thing became more and more evident the longer Issgazed: an artist, and one with a most scrupulous eye for form,sshad superintended all these arrangements. The greatest care hadssbeen taken to preserve a due medium between the neat and gracefulsson the one hand, and the pittoresque, in the true sense of thessItalian term, on the other. How hessmade it what it was—and why—with some particulars of Mr. Landorsshimself—may, possibly form the subject of another article.ssWILLIAM WILSONssWhat say of it? what say of CONSCIENCE grim,ssThat spectre in my path?ss—_Chamberlayne’s Pharronida._ssLet me call myself, for the present, William Wilson. The fairsspage now lying before me need not be sullied with my realssappellation. This has been already too much an object for thessscorn—for the horror—for the detestation of my race. To thessuttermost regions of the globe have not the indignant windsssbruited its unparalleled infamy? Oh, outcast of all outcasts mostssabandoned!—to the earth art thou not forever dead? to its honors,ssto its flowers, to its golden aspirations?—and a cloud, dense,ssdismal, and limitless, does it not hang eternally between thysshopes and heaven?ssI would not, if I could, here or to-day, embody a record of mysslater years of unspeakable misery, and unpardonable crime. Thisssepoch—these later years—took unto themselves a sudden elevationssin turpitude, whose origin alone it is my present purpose tossassign. Men usually grow base by degrees. From me, in an instant,ssall virtue dropped bodily as a mantle. From comparatively trivialsswickedness I passed, with the stride of a giant, into more thanssthe enormities of an Elah-Gabalus. What chance—what one eventssbrought this evil thing to pass, bear with me while I relate.ssDeath approaches; and the shadow which foreruns him has thrown asssoftening influence over my spirit. I long, in passing throughssthe dim valley, for the sympathy—I had nearly said for thesspity—of my fellow men. I would fain have them believe that I havessbeen, in some measure, the slave of circumstances beyond humansscontrol. I would wish them to seek out for me, in the details Issam about to give, some little oasis of fatality amid a wildernessssof error. I would have them allow—what they cannot refrain fromssallowing—that, although temptation may have erewhile existed asssgreat, man was never thus, at least, tempted before—certainly,ssnever thus fell. And is it therefore that he has never thussssuffered? Have I not indeed been living in a dream? And am I notssnow dying a victim to the horror and the mystery of the wildestssof all sublunary visions?ssI am the descendant of a race whose imaginative and easilyssexcitable temperament has at all times rendered them remarkable;ssand, in my earliest infancy, I gave evidence of having fullyssinherited the family character. As I advanced in years it wasssmore strongly developed; becoming, for many reasons, a cause ofssserious disquietude to my friends, and of positive injury tossmyself. I grew self-willed, addicted to the wildest caprices, andssa prey to the most ungovernable passions. Weak-minded, and besetsswith constitutional infirmities akin to my own, my parents couldssdo but little to check the evil propensities which distinguishedssme. Some feeble and ill-directed efforts resulted in completessfailure on their part, and, of course, in total triumph on mine.ssThenceforward my voice was a household law; and at an age whenssfew children have abandoned their leading-strings, I was left tossthe guidance of my own will, and became, in all but name, thessmaster of my own actions.ssMy earliest recollections of a school-life, are connected with asslarge, rambling, Elizabethan house, in a misty-looking village ofssEngland, where were a vast number of gigantic and gnarled trees,ssand where all the houses were excessively ancient. In truth, itsswas a dream-like and spirit-soothing place, that venerable oldsstown. At this moment, in fancy, I feel the refreshing chillinessssof its deeply-shadowed avenues, inhale the fragrance of itsssthousand shrubberies, and thrill anew with undefinable delight,ssat the deep hollow note of the church-bell, breaking, each hour,sswith sullen and sudden roar, upon the stillness of the duskyssatmosphere in which the fretted Gothic steeple lay imbedded andssasleep.ssIt gives me, perhaps, as much of pleasure as I can now in anyssmanner experience, to dwell upon minute recollections of thessschool and its concerns. Steeped in misery as I am—misery, alas!ssonly too real—I shall be pardoned for seeking relief, howeverssslight and temporary, in the weakness of a few rambling details.ssThese, moreover, utterly trivial, and even ridiculous inssthemselves, assume, to my fancy, adventitious importance, asssconnected with a period and a locality when and where I recognisessthe first ambiguous monitions of the destiny which afterwards sossfully overshadowed me. Let me then remember.ssThe house, I have said, was old and irregular. The grounds weressextensive, and a high and solid brick wall, topped with a bed ofssmortar and broken glass, encompassed the whole. This prison-likessrampart formed the limit of our domain; beyond it we saw butssthrice a week—once every Saturday afternoon, when, attended bysstwo ushers, we were permitted to take brief walks in a bodyssthrough some of the neighbouring fields—and twice during Sunday,sswhen we were paraded in the same formal manner to the morning andssevening service in the one church of the village. The teeming brain of childhood requires nossexternal world of incident to occupy or amuse it; and thessapparently dismal monotony of a school was replete with moressintense excitement than my riper youth has derived from luxury,ssor my full manhood from crime. Yet I must believe that my firstssmental development had in it much of the uncommon—even much ofssthe _outré_. Upon mankind at large the events of very earlyssexistence rarely leave in mature age any definite impression. Allssis gray shadow—a weak and irregular remembrance—an indistinctssregathering of feeble pleasures and phantasmagoric pains. With messthis is not so. This exception was found in the person of assscholar, who, although no relation, bore the same Christian andsssurname as myself;—a circumstance, in fact, little remarkable;ssfor, notwithstanding a noble descent, mine was one of thosesseveryday appellations which seem, by prescriptive right, to havessbeen, time out of mind, the common property of the mob. In thisssnarrative I have therefore designated myself as William Wilson,—assfictitious title not very dissimilar to the real. My namesakessalone, of those who in school phraseology constituted “our set,”sspresumed to compete with me in the studies of the class—in thesssports and broils of the play-ground—to refuse implicit belief inssmy assertions, and submission to my will—indeed, to interferesswith my arbitrary dictation in any respect whatsoever. If theressis on earth a supreme and unqualified despotism, it is thessdespotism of a master-mind in boyhood over the less energeticssspirits of its companions.ssWilson’s rebellion was to me a source of the greatestssembarrassment; the more so as, in spite of the bravado with whichssin public I made a point of treating him and his pretensions, Isssecretly felt that I feared him, and could not help thinking thessequality which he maintained so easily with myself, a proof ofsshis true superiority; since not to be overcome cost me assperpetual struggle. Yet this superiority—even this equality—wasssin truth acknowledged by no one but myself; our associates, bysssome unaccountable blindness, seemed not even to suspect it.ssIndeed, his competition, his resistance, and especially hisssimpertinent and dogged interference with my purposes, were notssmore pointed than private. He appeared to be destitute alike ofssthe ambition which urged, and of the passionate energy of mindsswhich enabled me to excel. I could onlyssconceive this singular behavior to arise from a consummatessself-conceit assuming the vulgar airs of patronage andssprotection.ssPerhaps it was this latter trait in Wilson’s conduct, conjoinedsswith our identity of name, and the mere accident of our havingssentered the school upon the same day, which set afloat the notionssthat we were brothers, among the senior classes in the academy.ssThese do not usually inquire with much strictness into thessaffairs of their juniors. I have before said, or should havesssaid, that Wilson was not, in the most remote degree, connectedsswith my family. But assuredly if we had been brothers we mustsshave been twins; for, after leaving Dr. Bransby’s, I casuallysslearned that my namesake was born on the nineteenth of January,ss1813—and this is a somewhat remarkable coincidence; for the dayssis precisely that of my own nativity.ssIt may seem strange that in spite of the continual anxietyssoccasioned me by the rivalry of Wilson, and his intolerablessspirit of contradiction, I could not bring myself to hate himssaltogether. We had, to be sure, nearly every day a quarrel insswhich, yielding me publicly the palm of victory, he, in somessmanner, contrived to make me feel that it was he who had deservedssit; yet a sense of pride on my part, and a veritable dignity onsshis own, kept us always upon what are called “speaking terms,”sswhile there were many points of strong congeniality in oursstempers, operating to awake me in a sentiment which our positionssalone, perhaps, prevented from ripening into friendship. It isssdifficult, indeed, to define, or even to describe, my realssfeelings towards him. They formed a motley and heterogeneousssadmixture;—some petulant animosity, which was not yet hatred,sssome esteem, more respect, much fear, with a world of uneasysscuriosity. To the moralist it will be unnecessary to say, inssaddition, that Wilson and myself were the most inseparable ofsscompanions.ssIt was no doubt the anomalous state of affairs existing betweenssus, which turned all my attacks upon him, (and they were many,sseither open or covert) into the channel of banter or practicalssjoke (giving pain while assuming the aspect of mere fun) ratherssthan into a more serious and determined hostility. But myssendeavours on this head were by no means uniformly successful,sseven when my plans were the most wittily concocted; for myssnamesake had much about him, in character, of that unassuming andssquiet austerity which, while enjoying the poignancy of its ownssjokes, has no heel of Achilles in itself, and absolutely refusesssto be laughed at. I could find, indeed, but one vulnerable point,ssand that, lying in a personal peculiarity, arising, perhaps, fromssconstitutional disease, would have been spared by any antagonistssless at his wit’s end than myself;—my rival had a weakness in thessfaucal or guttural organs, which precluded him from raising hisssvoice at any time above a very low whisper. Of this defect I didssnot fail to take what poor advantage lay in my power.ssWilson’s retaliations in kind were many; and there was one formssof his practical wit that disturbed me beyond measure. How hissssagacity first discovered at all that so petty a thing would vexssme, is a question I never could solve; but, having discovered, hesshabitually practised the annoyance. I had always felt aversion tossmy uncourtly patronymic, and its very common, if not plebeiansspraenomen. The words were venom in my ears; and when, upon thessday of my arrival, a second William Wilson came also to thessacademy, I felt angry with him for bearing the name, and doublyssdisgusted with the name because a stranger bore it, who would bessthe cause of its twofold repetition, who would be constantly inssmy presence, and whose concerns, in the ordinary routine of thessschool business, must inevitably, on account of the detestablesscoincidence, be often confounded with my own.ssThe feeling of vexation thus engendered grew stronger with everysscircumstance tending to show resemblance, moral or physical,ssbetween my rival and myself. I had not then discovered thessremarkable fact that we were of the same age; but I saw that wesswere of the same height, and I perceived that we were evensssingularly alike in general contour of person and outline ofssfeature. I was galled, too, by the rumor touching a relationship,sswhich had grown current in the upper forms. In a word, nothingsscould more seriously disturb me, (although I scrupulouslyssconcealed such disturbance,) than any allusion to a similarity ofssmind, person, or condition existing between us. But, in truth, Isshad no reason to believe that (with the exception of the matterssof relationship, and in the case of Wilson himself,) thissssimilarity had ever been made a subject of comment, or evenssobserved at all by our schoolfellows. That he observed it in allssits bearings, and as fixedly as I, was apparent; but that hesscould discover in such circumstances so fruitful a field ofssannoyance, can only be attributed, as I said before, to his moressthan ordinary penetration.ssHis cue, which was to perfect an imitation of myself, lay both insswords and in actions; and most admirably did he play his part. Myssdress it was an easy matter to copy; my gait and general mannersswere, without difficulty, appropriated; in spite of hisssconstitutional defect, even my voice did not escape him. Mysslouder tones were, of course, unattempted, but then the key—itsswas identical; _and his singular whisper, it grew the very echossof my own_.ssHow greatly this most exquisite portraiture harassed me, (for itsscould not justly be termed a caricature,) I will not now venturessto describe. The wretchedness of earth is multiform.ssOverreaching the wide horizon as the rainbow, its hues are asssvarious as the hues of that arch—as distinct too, yet asssintimately blended. Overreaching the wide horizon as the rainbow!ssHow is it that from beauty I have derived a type ofssunloveliness?—from the covenant of peace, a simile of sorrow? Butssas, in ethics, evil is a consequence of good, so, in fact, out ofssjoy is sorrow born. Our line has been calledssa race of visionaries; and in many striking particulars—in thesscharacter of the family mansion—in the frescos of the chiefsssaloon—in the tapestries of the dormitories—in the chiselling ofsssome buttresses in the armory—but more especially in the galleryssof antique paintings—in the fashion of the library chamber—and,sslastly, in the very peculiar nature of the library’ssscontents—there is more than sufficient evidence to warrant thessbelief.ssThe recollections of my earliest years are connected with thatsschamber, and with its volumes—of which latter I will say no more.ssHere died my mother. Herein was I born. But it is mere idlenessssto say that I had not lived before—that the soul has no previousssexistence. You deny it?—let us not argue the matter. Convincedssmyself, I seek not to convince. Men have called me mad; but the question is not yetsssettled, whether madness is or is not the loftiestssintelligence—whether much that is glorious—whether all that isssprofound—does not spring from disease of thought—from moods ofssmind exalted at the expense of the general intellect. They whossdream by day are cognizant of many things which escape those whossdream only by night. In their gray visions they obtain glimpsesssof eternity, and thrill, in awakening, to find that they havessbeen upon the verge of the great secret. In snatches, they learnsssomething of the wisdom which is of good, and more of the meressknowledge which is of evil. Thus it was that we lived all alone, knowing nothing ofssthe world without the valley—I, and my cousin, and her mother.ssFrom the dim regions beyond the mountains at the upper end of ourssencircled domain, there crept out a narrow and deep river,ssbrighter than all save the eyes of Eleonora; and, windingssstealthily about in mazy courses, it passed away, at length,ssthrough a shadowy gorge, among hills still dimmer than thosesswhence it had issued. We called it the “River of Silence”; forssthere seemed to be a hushing influence in its flow. No murmurssarose from its bed, and so gently it wandered along, that thesspearly pebbles upon which we loved to gaze, far down within itsssbosom, stirred not at all, but lay in a motionless content, eachssin its own old station, shining on gloriously forever.ssThe margin of the river, and of the many dazzling rivulets thatssglided through devious ways into its channel, as well as thessspaces that extended from the margins away down into the depthsssof the streams until they reached the bed of pebbles at thessbottom,—these spots, not less than the whole surface of thessvalley, from the river to the mountains that girdled it in, weresscarpeted all by a soft green grass, thick, short, perfectly even,ssand vanilla-perfumed, but so besprinkled throughout with thessyellow buttercup, the white daisy, the purple violet, and thessruby-red asphodel, that its exceeding beauty spoke to our heartsssin loud tones, of the love and of the glory of God.ssAnd, here and there, in groves about this grass, likesswildernesses of dreams, sprang up fantastic trees, whose tallssslender stems stood not upright, but slanted gracefully towardssthe light that peered at noon-day into the centre of the valley.ssTheir mark was speckled with the vivid alternate splendor ofssebony and silver, and was smoother than all save the cheeks ofssEleonora; so that, but for the brilliant green of the huge leavesssthat spread from their summits in long, tremulous lines, dallyingsswith the Zephyrs, one might have fancied them giant serpents ofssSyria doing homage to their sovereign the Sun.ssHand in hand about this valley, for fifteen years, roamed I withssEleonora before Love entered within our hearts. It was onessevening at the close of the third lustrum of her life, and of thessfourth of my own, that we sat, locked in each other’s embrace,ssbeneath the serpent-like trees, and looked down within the waterssof the River of Silence at our images therein. We spoke no wordsssduring the rest of that sweet day, and our words even upon thessmorrow were tremulous and few. We had drawn the God Eros fromssthat wave, and now we felt that he had enkindled within us thessfiery souls of our forefathers. The passions which had forsscenturies distinguished our race, came thronging with the fanciesssfor which they had been equally noted, and together breathed assdelirious bliss over the Valley of the Many-Colored Grass. Asschange fell upon all things. Strange, brilliant flowers,ssstar-shaped, burn out upon the trees where no flowers had beenssknown before. The tints of the green carpet deepened; and when,ssone by one, the white daisies shrank away, there sprang up inssplace of them, ten by ten of the ruby-red asphodel. And lifessarose in our paths; for the tall flamingo, hitherto unseen, withssall gay glowing birds, flaunted his scarlet plumage before us.ssThe golden and silver fish haunted the river, out of the bosom ofsswhich issued, little by little, a murmur that swelled, at length,ssinto a lulling melody more divine than that of the harp ofssÆolus—sweeter than all save the voice of Eleonora. And now, too,ssa voluminous cloud, which we had long watched in the regions ofssHesper, floated out thence, all gorgeous in crimson and gold, andsssettling in peace above us, sank, day by day, lower and lower,ssuntil its edges rested upon the tops of the mountains, turningssall their dimness into magnificence, and shutting us up, as ifssforever, within a magic prison-house of grandeur and of glory.ssThe loveliness of Eleonora was that of the Seraphim; but she wasssa maiden artless and innocent as the brief life she had led amongssthe flowers. No guile disguised the fervor of love which animatedssher heart, and she examined with me its inmost recesses as wesswalked together in the Valley of the Many-Colored Grass, andssdiscoursed of the mighty changes which had lately taken placesstherein.ssAt length, having spoken one day, in tears, of the last sadsschange which must befall Humanity, she thenceforward dwelt onlyssupon this one sorrowful theme, interweaving it into all ourssconverse, as, in the songs of the bard of Schiraz, the samessimages are found occurring, again and again, in every impressivessvariation of phrase.ssShe had seen that the finger of Death was upon her bosom—that,sslike the ephemeron, she had been made perfect in loveliness onlyssto die; but the terrors of the grave to her lay solely in assconsideration which she revealed to me, one evening at twilight,ssby the banks of the River of Silence. She grieved to think that,sshaving entombed her in the Valley of the Many-Colored Grass, Isswould quit forever its happy recesses, transferring the lovesswhich now was so passionately her own to some maiden of the outerssand everyday world. And, then and there, I threw myself hurriedlyssat the feet of Eleonora, and offered up a vow, to herself and tossHeaven, that I would never bind myself in marriage to anyssdaughter of Earth—that I would in no manner prove recreant to herssdear memory, or to the memory of the devout affection with whichssshe had blessed me. And I called the Mighty Ruler of the Universessto witness the pious solemnity of my vow. And the curse which Issinvoked of Him and of her, a saint in Helusion should I provesstraitorous to that promise, involved a penalty the exceedingssgreat horror of which will not permit me to make record of itsshere. And the bright eyes of Eleonora grew brighter at my words;ssand she sighed as if a deadly burthen had been taken from herssbreast; and she trembled and very bitterly wept; but she madessacceptance of the vow, (for what was she but a child?) and itssmade easy to her the bed of her death. And she said to me, notssmany days afterward, tranquilly dying, that, because of what Isshad done for the comfort of her spirit she would watch over me inssthat spirit when departed, and, if so it were permitted herssreturn to me visibly in the watches of the night; but, if thisssthing were, indeed, beyond the power of the souls in Paradise,ssthat she would, at least, give me frequent indications of hersspresence, sighing upon me in the evening winds, or filling thessair which I breathed with perfume from the censers of the angels.ssAnd, with these words upon her lips, she yielded up her innocentsslife, putting an end to the first epoch of my own.ssThus far I have faithfully said. But as I pass the barrier inssTime’s path, formed by the death of my beloved, and proceed withssthe second era of my existence, I feel that a shadow gathers overssmy brain, and I mistrust the perfect sanity of the record. Butsslet me on.—Years dragged themselves along heavily, and still Issdwelled within the Valley of the Many-Colored Grass; but a secondsschange had come upon all things. The star-shaped flowers shrankssinto the stems of the trees, and appeared no more. The tints ofssthe green carpet faded; and, one by one, the ruby-red asphodelssswithered away; and there sprang up, in place of them, ten by ten,ssdark, eye-like violets, that writhed uneasily and were everssencumbered with dew. And Life departed from our paths; for thesstall flamingo flaunted no longer his scarlet plumage before us,ssbut flew sadly from the vale into the hills, with all the gayssglowing birds that had arrived in his company. And the golden andsssilver fish swam down through the gorge at the lower end of ourssdomain and bedecked the sweet river never again. And the lullingssmelody that had been softer than the wind-harp of Æolus, and moressdivine than all save the voice of Eleonora, it died little bysslittle away, in murmurs growing lower and lower, until the streamssreturned, at length, utterly, into the solemnity of its originalsssilence. And then, lastly, the voluminous cloud uprose, and,ssabandoning the tops of the mountains to the dimness of old, fellssback into the regions of Hesper, and took away all its manifoldssgolden and gorgeous glories from the Valley of the Many-ColoredssGrass.ssYet the promises of Eleonora were not forgotten; for I heard thesssounds of the swinging of the censers of the angels; and streamsssof a holy perfume floated ever and ever about the valley; and atsslone hours, when my heart beat heavily, the winds that bathed myssbrow came unto me laden with soft sighs; and indistinct murmursssfilled often the night air, and once—oh, but once only! I wasssawakened from a slumber, like the slumber of death, by thesspressing of spiritual lips upon my own.ssBut the void within my heart refused, even thus, to be filled. Isslonged for the love which had before filled it to overflowing. Atsslength the valley pained me through its memories of Eleonora, andssI left it for ever for the vanities and the turbulent triumphs ofssthe world.ssI found myself within a strange city, where all things might havessserved to blot from recollection the sweet dreams I had dreamedssso long in the Valley of the Many-Colored Grass. The pomps andsspageantries of a stately court, and the mad clangor of arms, andssthe radiant loveliness of women, bewildered and intoxicated myssbrain. But as yet my soul had proved true to its vows, and thessindications of the presence of Eleonora were still given me inssthe silent hours of the night. Suddenly these manifestations theyssceased, and the world grew dark before mine eyes, and I stoodssaghast at the burning thoughts which possessed, at the terriblesstemptations which beset me; for there came from some far, farssdistant and unknown land, into the gay court of the king Issserved, a maiden to whose beauty my whole recreant heart yieldedssat once—at whose footstool I bowed down without a struggle, inssthe most ardent, in the most abject worship of love. What,ssindeed, was my passion for the young girl of the valley insscomparison with the fervor, and the delirium, and thessspirit-lifting ecstasy of adoration with which I poured out mysswhole soul in tears at the feet of the ethereal Ermengarde?—Oh,ssbright was the seraph Ermengarde! and in that knowledge I hadssroom for none other. Oh, divine was the angel Ermengarde! and asssI looked down into the depths of her memorial eyes, I thoughtssonly of them—and of her.ssI wedded—nor dreaded the curse I had invoked; and its bitternesssswas not visited upon me. And once—but once again in the silencessof the night; there came through my lattice the soft sighs whichsshad forsaken me; and they modelled themselves into familiar andsssweet voice, saying:ss“Sleep in peace! for the Spirit of Love reigneth and ruleth, and,ssin taking to thy passionate heart her who is Ermengarde, thou artssabsolved, for reasons which shall be made known to thee inssHeaven, of thy vows unto Eleonora.”
